Fact	West Bengal : <NAME> and <NAME> The following Judgments were delivered:  SARKARIA, J.
Fact	This reference to <ORG> raises a question in regard to the constitutional validity of death penalty for murder provided in Section 302 , Penal Code , and the sentencing procedure embodied in sub-section (3) of Section 354 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973.
Fact	The reference has arisen in these circumstances: <NAME>, appellant in Criminal Appeal No.
Fact	273 of 1979, was tried and convicted and sentenced to death under Section 302 , Indian Penal Code for the murders of <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME> by the Sessions Judge.
Fact	<ORG> confirmed his death sentence and dismi ssed his appeal.
Fact	<NAME> appeal by special leave, came up for hearing before a Bench of this Court (consisting of <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.).
Fact	The only question for consideration in the appeal was, whether the facts found by the Courts below would be "special reasons" for awarding the death sentence as required under Section 354(3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1973.
Fact	<NAME>, appearing as Amicus Curiae on behalf of the appellant, <NAME>, in Criminal Appeal No.
Precedent	273 of 1979.
Argument	164  contended that in view of the ratio of <CITATION_1309719> .,(1) the Courts below were not competent to impose the extreme penalty of death on the appellant.
Argument	It was submitted that neither the circumstance that the appellant was previously convicted for murder and committed these murder after he had served out the life sentence in the earlier case, not the fact that these three murders were extremely heinous and inhuman, constitutes a "special reason" for imposing the death sentence within the meaning of Sectio n 354(3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1973.
Argument	Reliance for this argument was placed on <NAME> (ibid) which according to the counsel, was on facts very similar, if not identical, to that case.
Argument	<NAME>, J. was of opinion that the majority view in <NAME> taken by <NAME>, who spoke for himself and <NAME>, was contrary to the judgment of <ORG> in <CITATION_71095988> (2), inter alia, on these aspects:  (i) In <NAME>, <NAME>.
Argument	observed :  "The main focus of our judgment is on this poignant gap in 'human rights jurisprudence' within the limits of the Penal Code , impregnated by the Constitution.
Argument	To put it pithily, a world order voicing the worth of the human person, a cultural legacy charged with compassion, an interpretative liberation from colonial callousness to life and liberty, a concern for social justice as setting the sights of individual justice, interest with the inherited text of the Penal Code to yield the goals desiderate d by the Preamble and Articles 14, 19 and 21."
Argument	According to <NAME>, J., the challenge to the award of the death sentence as violative of Articles 19, 14 and 21, was repelled by <ORG> in <NAME>'s case.
Argument	(ii) In <NAME>'s case, <ORG> held:  "The impossibility of laying down standards (in the matter of sentencing) is at the very core of criminal law as administered in India which invests the judges with a very wide discretion in the matter of fixing the degree of punishment and that this discretion in the matter of sentence in liable to be corrected by superior <ORG>...
Argument	The exercise of judicial discretion on well recognised principles is, in the final analysis, the safest possible safeguard for the accused."
Precedent	In <NAME>, the majority decision characterised the above observations in <NAME> as: "incidental observations without concentration on the sentencing criteria", and said that they are not the ratio of the decision, adding.
Precedent	"Judgments are not Bible for every  line to be venerated."
Precedent	(iii) In <NAME>, the plurality observed: "It is constitutionally permissible to swing a criminal out of corporeal existence only if the security of <ORG> and society, public order and the interests of the general public compel that course as provided in Article 19(2) to (6)."
Precedent	This view again, according to <NAME>, J., is inconsistent with the law laid down by <ORG> in Jagmohan, wherein it was held that deprivation of life is constitutionally permissible if that is done according to "procedure established by law".
Precedent	(iv) In <NAME>, the majority has further opined:  "The only correct approach is to read into Section  302.
Precedent	I.P.C .
Precedent	and Section 354(3) Cr.
Precedent	P.C., the human rights and humane trends in the Constitution.
Precedent	So examined, the rights to life and the fundamental freedoms is deprived when he is hanged to death, his dignity is defiled when his neck is noosed and strangled."
Precedent	Against the above, <NAME>, J. commented : 'The only change after the Consti tution Bench delivered its judgment is the introduction of Section 354(3) which requires special reasons to be given if the <ORG> is to award the death sentence.
Precedent	If without the restriction of stating sufficient reasons death sentence could be constitutionally awarded under the I.P.C .
Precedent	and Cr.
Precedent	P.C.
Precedent	as it stood before the amendment, it is difficult to perceive how by requiring special reasons to be given the amended section would be unconstitutional unless the "sentencing sector is made most restrictive and least vagarious".
Precedent	(v) In <NAME>, the majority has held that: "Such extraordinary grounds alone constitutionally qualify as special reasons as leave on option to the <ORG> but to execute the offender if <ORG> and society are to survive.
Precedent	One stroke of murder hardly qualifies for this drastic requirement, however, gruesome the killing or pathetic the situation, unless the inherent testimony coming from that act is irresistible that the murderous appetite of the convict is too chronic and deadly that o rdered life in a given locality or society or in prison itself would be gone if this man were now or later to be at large.
Precedent	If he is an irredeemable, like a bloodthirsty tiger, he has to quit his terrestrial tenancy."
Precedent	According to <NAME>, J., what is extracted above, runs directly counter to and cannot be reconciled with the following observations in <NAME>'s case:  "But some (murders) at least are diabolical in conception and cruel in execution.
Precedent	In some others where the victim is a person of high standing in the country, society is liable to be recked to its very foundation.
Precedent	Such murders cannot be simply wished away by finding alibis in the social maladjustment of the murderer.
Precedent	Prevalence of such crimes speaks, in the opinion of many, for the inevitability of death penalty not only by way of deterrence but as a token of emphatic disapproval by the society A very responsible body (<ORG>) has come to the conclusion after considering all the relevant factors.
Precedent	On the conclusions thus offered to us,  it will be difficult to hold that capital punishment as such is unreasonable or not required in the public interest."
Precedent	(vi) <NAME>, J. was further of the opinion that it is equally beyond the functions of a <ORG> to evolve "working rules for imposition of death sentence bearing the markings of enlightened flexibility and social sensibility" or to make law "by cross-fertilisation from sociology, history, cultural anthropology and current national perils and developmental goals and, above all, constitutional currents".
Argument	This function, in his view, belongs only to <ORG>.
Argument	The <ORG> must administer the law as it stands.
Argument	(vii) The learned Judge has further expressed that the view taken by <NAME>, J. in <NAME> that " 'special reasons' necessary for imposing death penalty must relate not to the crime as such, but to the criminal" is not warranted by the law as it stands today.
Precedent	Without expressing his own opinion on the various questions raised in that case including the one with regard to t he scope, amplification and application of Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1974, Sarkaria, J., in agreement with <NAME>, J., directed the records of the case to be submitted to the Hon'ble the Chief Justice, for constituting a large <ORG> "to resolve the doubts, difficulties and inconsistencies pointed out by <NAME>, J."
Precedent	In the meanwhile, several persons convicted of murders and sentenced to death, filed writ petitions (namely, Writ Petitions 564, 165, 179, 434, 89, 754, 756 and 976 of 1979) under Article 32 of the Constitution directly challenging the constitutional validity of the death penalty provided in Section 302 of the Indian Penal Code for the offence of murder, and the sentencing procedure provided in Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1974.
Precedent	That is how, the matter has now come up before this larger <ORG> of five Judges.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the outset, <NAME> submitted with some vehemance and persistence, that <NAME>'s case needs reconsideration by a larger <ORG> if  not by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reconsideration of <NAME>, according to the learned counsel, is necessitated because of subsequent events and changes in law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, it is pointed out that when <NAME> was decided in 1972, the then extant Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 left the choice between death and life imprisonment as punishment for murder entirely to the discretion of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This position has since undergone a complete change and under Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973, death sentence has ceased to be the normal penalty for murder.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, it is argued, the seven-Judge decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_1766147> (1) has given a new interpretative dimension of the provisions of Articles 21, 19 and 14 and their inter-relationship, and according to this new interpretation every law of punitive detention both in its procedural and substantive aspects must pass the test of all the three Articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is stressed that an argument founded on this expansive interpretation of these Arti cles was not available when <NAME> was decided.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, it is submitted that India has since acceded to the international Covenant of Civil and Political Rights adopted by <ORG>, which came into force in December 16, 1976.
RatioOfTheDecision	By virtue of this Covenant.
RatioOfTheDecision	India and the other 47 countries who are a party to it, stand committed to a policy for abolition of the 'death penalty'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dr. <NAME> submitted that the question of death penalty cannot be foreclosed for ever on the abstract doctrine of stare decisis by a previous decision of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is emphasised that the very nature of the problem is such that it must be the subject of review from time to time so as to be in tune with the evolving standards of decency in a maturing society.
RulingByPresentCourt	The learned Solicitor-General, <NAME> opposed the request of <NAME> for referring the matter to a larger <ORG> because such a course would only mean avoidable delay in disposal of the matter.
RulingByPresentCourt	At the same time, the learned co unsel made it clear that since the constitutionality of the death penalty for murder was now sought to be challenged on additional arguments based on subsequent events and changes in law, he would have no objection on the ground of stare decisis, to a fresh consideration of the whole problem by this very <ORG>.
RulingByPresentCourt	In view of the concession made by <NAME>, we proceeded to hear the counsel for the parties at length, and to deal afresh with the constitutional questions concerning death penalty raised in these writ petitions.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have heard the arguments of <NAME> appearing for the writ-petitioners in Writ Petition No.
RatioOfTheDecision	564/79 for more than three weeks and also those of Dr. <NAME>, Dr. <NAME> and <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, appearing for interveners or for the other writ-petitioners.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have also heard the arguments of <NAME>, Solicitor-General, appearing for <ORG> and <NAME> appearing for the State of Maharashtra and the other counsel appearing for the res pondents.
Fact	The principal questions that fall to be considered in this case are:  (I) Whether death penalty provided for the offence of murder in Section 302 , Penal Code is unconstitutional.
Fact	(II) If the answer to the foregoing question be in the negative, whether the sentencing procedure provided in Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973 (Act 2 of 1974) is unconstitutional on the ground that it invests the <ORG> with unguided and untrammelled discretion and allows death sentence to be arbitrarily or freakishly imposed on a person found guilty of murder or any other capital offence punishable under the Indian Penal Code with death or, in the alternative, with imprisonment for life.
RulingByPresentCourt	We will first take up Question No.
RulingByPresentCourt	(I) relating to the constitutional validity of Section 302 , Penal Code .
Fact	Question No.
Fact	(I):  Before dealing with the contentions canvassed, it will be useful to have a short survey of the legislative history of the provisions of the Penal Code which permit the imposition of death  penalty for certain offences.
Fact	The Indian Penal Code was drafted by <ORG> presided over by Mr. <NAME>.
Fact	The draft underwent further revision at the hands of well-known jurists, like Sir <NAME>, and was completed in 1850.
Fact	The Indian Penal Code was passed by the then <ORG> on October 6, 1860 and was enacted as Act No XLV of 1860.
Statute	Section 53 of the Penal Code enumerates punishments to which offenders are liable under the provisions of this Code.
Statute	Clause Firstly of the Section mentions 'Death' as one of such punishments.
Statute	Regarding 'death' as a punishment, the authors of the Code say: "We are convinced that it ought to be very sparingly inflicted, and we propose to employ it only in cases where either murder or the highest offence against the <ORG> has been committed."
Statute	Accordingly, under the Code , death is the punishment that must be awarded for murder by a person under sentence of imprisonment for life ( Section 303 ).
Statute	This apart, the Penal Code prescribes 'death' as an  alternative punishment to which the offenders may be sentenced, for the following seven offences:  121) (2) Abetting mutiny actually committed.
Statute	(s. 132) (3) Giving or fabricating false evidence upon which an innocent person suffers death.
Statute	(s. 194) (4) Murder which may be punished with death or life imprisonment.
Statute	(s. 302) (5) Abetment of suicide of a minor or insane, or intoxicated person.
Statute	(s. 305) (6) Dacoity accompanied with murder.
Statute	(s. 396) (7) Attempt to murder by a person under sentence of imprisonment for life if hurt is caused.
Fact	(s. 307) In the instant cases, the impugned provision of the Indian Penal Code is Section 302 which says: "Whoever commits murder shall be punished with death, or imprisonment for life, and also be liable to fine."
Fact	The related provisions are contained in Sections 299 and 300 .
Statute	Section 299 defines 'culpable homicide'.
Statute	Section 300 defines 'murder'.
Statute	Its material part runs as follows:  "Except in the cases hereinafter excepted, culpable homicide is murder, if the act by which the de ath is caused is done with the intention of causing death, or Secondly-If it is done with the intention of causing such bodily injury as the offender knows to be likely to cause death of the person to whom the harm is caused, or Thirdly-If it is done with the intention of causing bodily injury to any person and the bodily injury intended to be inflicted is sufficient in the ordinary course of nature to cause death, or Fourthly-If the person committing the act knows that it is so imminently dangerous that it must, in all probability, cause death, or such bodily injury as is likely to cause death, and commits, such act without any excuse for incurring the risk of causing death or such injury as aforesaid."
Argument	The first contention of <NAME> is that the provision of death penalty in Section 302 , Penal Code offends Article 19 of the Constitution.
Argument	It is submitted that the right to live is basic to the enjoyment of all the six freedoms guaranteed in clauses (a) to (e) and (g) of Article 19 (1) of the Constitution  and death penalty puts an end to all these freedoms: that since death penalty serves no social purpose and its value as a deterrent remains unproven and it defiles the dignity of the individual so solemnly vouchsafed in the Preamble of the Constitution, its imposition must be regarded as an 'unreasonable restriction' amounting to total prohibition, on the six freedoms guaranteed in Article 19 (1).
Statute	Article 19 , as in force today, reads as under: "19 (1).
Statute	All citizens shall have the right-  (a) to freedom of speech and expression;  (b) to assemble peaceably and without arms;  (c) to form associations or unions;  (d) to move freely throughout the territory of India;  (e) to reside and settle in any part of the territory of India;  (f) .....................;  172  (g) to practice any profession, or to carry on any occupation, trade or business.
Statute	(2) Nothing in sub-clause (a) of clause (1) shall affect the operation of any existing law, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law, in so far as such law imposes rea sonable restrictions on the exercise of the right conferred by the said sub-clause in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India, the security of the <ORG>, friendly relations with foreign <ORG>s, public order, decency or morality, or in relation to contempt of court, defamation or incitement to an offence.
Statute	(3) Nothing in sub-clause (b) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India or public order, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right conferred by the said sub-clause.
Statute	(4) Nothing in sub-clause (c) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of the sovereignty and integrity of India or public order or morality, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right conferred by the said sub-clause.
Statute	(5) Nothing in su b-clauses (d) and (e) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevents the <ORG> from making any law imposing, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of any of the rights conferred by the said sub-clauses either in the interests of the general public or for the protection of the interests of any Scheduled Tribe.
Statute	(6) Nothing in sub-clause (g) of the said clause shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it imposes, or prevents the <ORG> from making any law imposing, in the interests of the general public, reasonable restrictions on the exercise of the right con-  173  ferred by the said sub-clause, and in particular, nothing in the said sub-clause, shall affect the operation of any existing law in so far as it relates to, or prevent the <ORG> from making any law relating to,-  (i) the professional or technical qualifications necessary for practising any profession or carrying on any occupation, trade or business, or  (ii) the carying on b y the <ORG>, or by a corporation owned or controlled by the <ORG>, of any trade, business, industry or service, whether to the exclusion, complete or partial, of citizens or otherwise."
Statute	It will be seen that the first part of the Article declares the rights in clause (1) comprising of six sub- clauses namely, (a) to (e) and (g).
Statute	The second part of the Article in its five clauses (2) to (6) specifies the limits upto which the abridgement of the rights declared in one or more of the sub-clauses of clause (1), may be permitted.
Statute	Broadly speaking, Article 19 is intended to protect the rights to the freedoms specifically enumerated in the six sub-clauses of clause (1) against <ORG> action, other than in the legitimate exercise of its power to regulate these rights in the public interest relating to heads specified in clauses (2) to (6).
RatioOfTheDecision	The six fundamental freedoms guaranteed under Article 19 (1) are not absolute rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, they are subject to inherent restraints stemming from the reciprocal obligation of one  member of a civil society to so use his rights as not to infringe or injure similar rights of another.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is on the principle sic utere tuo ut alienum non laedas.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, under clauses (2) to (6) these rights have been expressly made subject to the power of the <ORG> to impose reasonable restrictions, which may even extend to prohibition, on the exercise of those rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power, if properly exercised, is itself a safeguard of the freedoms guaranteed in clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	The conferment of this power is founded on the fundamental truth that uncontrolled liberty entirely freed from restraint, degenerates into a licence, leading to anarchy and chaos; that libertine pursuit of liberty, absolutely free, and free for all, may mean liberticide for all.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Liberty has, therefore," as Justice <NAME> put it, "to be limited in order to be effectively possessed."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is important to note that whereas Article 21 expressly deals with the right to life and personal liberty, Article 19 does not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to life i s not one of the rights mentioned in Article 19 (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	The first point under Question (1) to be considered is whether Article 19 is at all applicable for judging the validity of the impugned provision in Section 302 , Penal Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	As rightly pointed out by <NAME>, the condition precedent for the applicability of Article 19 is that the activity which the impugned law prohibits and penalises, must be within the purview and protection of Article 19 (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus considered, can any one say that he has a legal right or fundamental freedom under Article 19 (1) to practise the profession of a hired assassin or to form associations or unions or engage in a conspiracy with the object of committing murders or dacoities.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument that the provisions of the Penal Code , prescribing death sentence as an alternative penalty for murder have to be tested on the ground of Article 19 , appears to proceed on the fallacy that the freedoms guaranteed by Article 19 (1) are absolute freedoms and they cannot be curtaile d by law imposing reasonable restrictions, which may amount to total prohibition.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an argument was advanced before <ORG> in <CITATION_212098> .
Argument	(1) In that case the constitutional validity of certain provisions of the Bombay Lotteries and Prize Competition Control Act, 1952, as amended by Bombay Act No.
Argument	XXX of 1952, was challenged on the ground, inter alia, that it infringes the fundamental rights of the promoters of such competitions under Article 19 (1) (g ), to carry on their trade or business and that the restrictions imposed by the said Act cannot possibly be supported as reasonable restrictions in the interest of the general public permissible under Article 19 (b).
Argument	It was contended that the words "trade" or "business" or "commerce" in sub-clause (g) of Article 19 (a) should be read in their widest amplitude as any activity which is undertaken or carried on with a view to earning profit since there is nothing in Article 19 (1) (g) which may qualify or cut down the meaning of the criti cal words; that there is no justification for excluding from the meaning of those words activities which may be looked upon with disfavour by the <ORG> or the <ORG> as injurious to public morality or public interest.
Argument	Speaking for <ORG>, <NAME>, C.J.
RatioOfTheDecision	repelled this contention, in these terms:  "On this argument it will follow that criminal activities undertaken and carried on with a view to earning profit will be protected as fundamental rights until they are restricted by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus there will be a guaranteed right to carry on a business of hiring out goondas to commit assault or even murder, or house- breaking, or selling obscene pictures, of trafficking in women and so on until the law curbs or stops such activities.
RatioOfTheDecision	This appears to us to be completely unrealistic and incongruous.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have no doubt that there are certain activities which can under no circumstance be regarded as trade or business or commerce although the usual forms and instruments are employed therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	To exclude those activ ities from the meaning of those words is not to cut down their meaning at all but to say only that they are not within the true meaning of those words."
RatioOfTheDecision	This approach to the problem still holds the field.
Precedent	The observations in Chamarbaugwala, extracted above, were recently quoted with approval by <NAME>, J., while delivering the judgment of the <ORG> in <CITATION_1547238> (1).
Precedent	<CITATION_1857950> (2), all the six learned Judges constituting the <ORG> held that punitive detention or imprisonment awarded as punishment after conviction for an offence under the Indian Penal Code is outside the scope of Article 19 , although this conclusion was reached by them by adopting more or less different approaches to the problem.
Argument	It was contended on behalf of <NAME> that since the preventive detention order results in the detention of the detenu in a cell, his rights specified in clauses (a) to  (e) and (g) of Article 19 (1) have been infringed.
Argument	176  <NAME>, <NAME> rejected this argument, inter alia, on th ese grounds:  (i) Argument would have been equally applicable to a case of punitive detention, and its acceptance would lead to absurd results.
RatioOfTheDecision	"In spite of the saving clauses (2) to (6), permitting abridgement of the rights connected with each other, punitive detention under several sections of the Penal Code , e.g.
RatioOfTheDecision	for theft, cheating, forgery and even ordinary assault, will be illegal, (because the reasonable restrictions in the interest of "public order" mentioned in clauses (2) to (4) of the Article would not cover these offences and many other crimes under the Penal Code which injure specific individuals and do not affect the community or the public at large).
RatioOfTheDecision	Unless such conclusion necessarily follows from the article, it is obvious that such construction should be avoided.
Precedent	In my opinion, such result is clearly not the outcome of the Constitution."
Precedent	(The underlined words within brackets supplied.)
Precedent	(At page 100 of the Report)  (ii) Judged by the test of direct and indirect effect on the rights referred  to in article 19 (1 ), the Penal Code is not a law imposing restrictions on these rights.
Precedent	The test is that "the legislation to be examined must be directly in respect of one of the rights mentioned in the sub-clauses.
Precedent	If there is a legislation directly attempting to control a citizen's freedom of speech or expression or his right to assemble peaceably and without arms, etc., the question whether that legislation is saved by the relevant saving clause of Article 19 will arise.
Precedent	If, however, the legislation is not directly in respect of any of these subjects, but as a result of the operation of other legislation, for instance, for punitive or preventive detention, his right under any of these sub- clauses is abridged, the question of the application of Article 19 does not arise.
Precedent	The true approach is only to consider the directness of the legislation and not what will be the result of the detention otherwise valid, on the mode of the detenu's life."
Precedent	(Pages 100-101).
Precedent	177  (iii)"The contents and subject-matter of  articles 19 and 21 are thus not the same..." (Page 105). "
Precedent	Article 19 (5) cannot apply to a substantive law depriving a citizen of personal liberty."
Precedent	" Article 19 (1) does not purport to cover all aspects of liberty or of personal liberty.
Precedent	Personal liberty would primarily mean liberty of the physical body.
Precedent	The rights given under article 19 (1) do not directly come under that description.
Precedent	In that Article only certain phases of liberty are dealt with".
Precedent	(Page 106) "In my opinion therefore, Article 19 should be read as a separate complete Article".
Precedent	(Page 107).
Precedent	<NAME>, also, opined "that lawful deprivation of personal liberty on conviction and sentence for committing a crime, or by a lawful order of preventive detention is "not within the purview of Article 19 at all, but is dealt with by the succeeding Articles 20 and 21."
Precedent	(Page 192).
Precedent	In tune with <NAME>, C.J., the learned Judge observed: "A construction which would bring within Article 19 imprisonment in punishment of a crime committed or in preven tion of a crime threatened would, as it seems to me, make a reductio ad absurdum of that provision.
Precedent	If imprisonment were to be regarded as a 'restriction' of the right mentioned in article 19 (1) (d ), it would equally be a restriction on the rights mentioned by the other sub-clauses of clause (1), with the result that all penal laws providing for imprisonment as a mode of punishment would have to run the gauntlet of clauses (2) to (6) before their validity could be accepted.
Precedent	For instance, the law which imprisons for theft would on that view, fall to be justified under clause (2) as a law sanctioning restriction of freedom of speech and expression."
Precedent	(Page 192).  "
Precedent	Article 19 confers the rights therein specified only on the citizens of India, while article 21 extends the protection of life and personal liberty to all persons citizens and non-citizens alike.
Precedent	Thus, the two Articles do not operate in a coterminous field."
Precedent	(Page 193).
Precedent	"(Personal liberty) was used in Article 21 as a sense which excludes the freedo ms dealt in Article 19 ....."   Rejecting the argument of the Attorney General, the learned Judge held that clauses (4) to (7) of Article 22 do not form a complete Code and that "the language of Article 21 is perfectly general and covers deprivation of personal liberty or incarceration, both for punitive and preventive reasons."
Precedent	(Page 207).
Precedent	<NAME>, J., however, adopted a different approach.
Precedent	In his judgment, "an examination of the provisions of Article 22 clearly suggests that the intention was to make it self- contained as regards the law of preventive detention and that the validity of a law on the subject of preventive detention cannot be examined or controlled either by the provisions of Article 21 or by the provisions of Article 19(5)."
Precedent	(Page 229).
Precedent	<NAME>, J. explained the relative scope of the Articles in this group, thus: "To me it seems that Article 19 of the Constitution gives a list of individual liberties and prescribes in the various clauses the restraints that may be placed upon them by law s o that they may not conflict with public welfare or general morality.
Precedent	On the other hand, Articles 20, 21 and 22 are primarily concerned with penal enactments or other laws under which personal safety or liberty of persons could be taken away in the interests of the society and they set down the limits within which the <ORG> control should be exercised.
Precedent	In my opinion, the group of articles 20 to 22 embody the entire protection guaranteed by the Constitution in relation to deprivation of life and personal liberty both with regard to substantive as well as to procedural law."
Precedent	(Page 255).
Precedent	"The only proper way of avoiding these anomalies is to interpret the two provisions (articles 19 and 21) as applying to different subjects.
Precedent	It is also unnecessary to enter into a discussion on the question...as to whether article 22 by itself is a self-contained Code with regard to the law of Preventive Detention."
Precedent	(Page 257).
Precedent	<NAME>, J., also, rejected the argument that the whole of the Indian Penal Code is a law imposing r easonable restriction on the rights conferred by Article 19 (1 ), with these observations (at Page 303) :  "To say that every crime undermines the security of the <ORG> and, therefore, every section of the Indian Penal Code , irrespective of whether it has any reference to speech or expression, is a law within the meaning of this clause is wholly unconvincing and betrays only a vain and forlorn attempt to find an explanation for meeting the argument that any conviction by a Court of law must necessarily infringe article 19 (1) (a).
Precedent	There can be no getting away from the fact that a detention as a result of a conviction impairs the freedom of speech for beyond what is permissible under clause (2) of article 19.
Precedent	Likewise, a detention on lawful conviction impairs each of the other personal rights mentioned in sub-clauses (3) to (6).
Precedent	The argument that every section of the Indian Penal Code irrespective of whether it has any reference to any of the rights referred to in sub- clauses (b) to (e) and (g) is a law impo <NAME> reasonable restriction on those several rights has not even the merit of plausibility.
Precedent	There can be no doubt that a detention as a result of lawful conviction must necessarily impair the fundamental personal rights guaranteed by article 19 (1) far beyond what is permissible under clauses (2) to (6) of that article and yet nobody can think of questioning the validity of the detention or of the section of the Indian Penal Code under which the sentence was passed."
Precedent	(ii) <NAME>, J. then gave an additional reason as to why validity of punitive detention or of the sections of the Penal Code under which the sentence was passed, cannot be challenged on the ground of article 19 , thus :  "Because the freedom of his person having been lawfully taken away, the convict ceases to be entitled to exercise .. any of the .. rights protected by clause (1) of article 19."
Precedent	(iii) The learned Judge also held that " article 19 protects some of the important attributes of personal liberty as independent rights and the expressio n 'personal liberty' has been used in article 21 as a compendious term including within its meaning all the varieties of rights which go to make up the personal liberties of men."
Precedent	(Page 299).
Precedent	<NAME>, J. dissented from the majority.
Precedent	In his opinion: "It cannot be said that articles 19, 20, 21 and 22 do not to some extent overlap each other.
Precedent	The case of a person who is convicted of an offence will come under article 20 and 21 and also under article 22 so far as his arrest and detention in custody before trial are concerned.
Precedent	Preventive detention, which is dealt with in article 22 , also amounts to deprivation of personal liberty which is referred to in article 19 (1)   (d)."
Precedent	(Page 148).
Precedent	<NAME>, J. held that since preventive detention, unlike punitive detention, directly infringes the right under Article 19(1)(d ), it must pass the test of clause (5).
Precedent	According to the learned Judge, only those laws are required to be tested on the anvil of Article 19 which directly restrict any of the rights guaranteed in  Article 19(1).
Precedent	Applying this test (of direct and indirect effect) to the provisions of the Indian Penal Code , the learned Judge pointed out that the Code "does not primarily or necessarily impose restrictions on the freedom of movement, and it is not correct to say that it is a law imposing restrictions on the right to move freely.
Precedent	Its primary object is to punish crime and not to restrict movement.
Precedent	The punishment may consist in imprisonment or a pecuniary penalty.
Precedent	If it consists in a pecuniary penalty, it obviously involves no restriction on movement, but if it consists in imprisonment, there is a restriction on movement.
Precedent	This restraint is imposed not under a law imposing restrictions on movement but under a law defining crime and making it punishable.
Precedent	The punishment is correlated with the violation of some other person's right and not with the right of movement possessed by the offender himself.
Precedent	In my opinion, therefore, the Indian Penal Code does not come within the ambit of the words "law imposing restric tion on the right to move freely."
Precedent	(Pages 145-146).
Precedent	In applying the above test, which was the same as adopted by <NAME>, C.J., <NAME>, J. reached a conclusion contrary to that reached by the Chief Justice, on the following reasoning ;  "Punitive detention is however essentially different from preventive detention.
Precedent	A person is punitively detained only after trial for committing a crime and after his guilt has been established in a competent court of justice.
Precedent	A person so convicted can take his case to <ORG> and sometimes bring it to this Court also; and he can in the course of the proceedings connected with his trial take all pleas available to him including the plea of want of jurisdiction of the Court of trial and the invalidity of the law under which he has been prosecuted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The final judgment in the criminal trial will thus constitute a serious obstacle in his way if he chooses to assert even after his conviction that his right under article 19(1)(d) has been violated.
RatioOfTheDecision	But a person who is  preventively detained has not to face such an obstacle whatever other obstacle may be in his way."
RatioOfTheDecision	(Page 146) We have copiously extracted from the judgments in <NAME> case, to show that all the propositions propounded, arguments and reasons employed or approaches adopted by the learned Judges in that case, in reaching the conclusion that the Indian Penal Code , particularly those of its provisions which do not have a direct impact on the rights conferred by Article 19(1 ), is not a law imposing restrictions on those rights, have not been overruled or rendered bad by the subsequent pronouncements of this <ORG> in Bank Nationalizaton(1) case or in <NAME> case.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, the proposition laid down by <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, and <NAME>, J.J. that the Indian Penal Code particularly those of its provisions which cannot be justified on the ground on reasonableness with reference to any of the specified heads, such as "public order" in clauses (2), (3) and (4), is not a law i mposing restrictions on any of the rights conferred by Article 19(1 ), still holds the field.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, the reasoning, explicit, or implicit in the judgments of <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME> that such a construction which treats every section of the Indian Penal Code as a law imposing 'restriction' on the rights in Article 19(1 ), will lead to absurdity is unassailable.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are several offences under the Penal Code , such as theft, cheating, ordinary assault, which do not violate or effect 'public order,' 'but only law and order'.
RatioOfTheDecision	These offences injure only specific individuals as distinguished from the public at large.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is by now settled that 'public order' means 'even tempo of the life of the community.'
RatioOfTheDecision	That being so, even all murders do not disturb or affect 'public order'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some murders may be of purely private significance and the injury or harm resulting therefrom affects only specific individuals and, consequently, such murders may not be covered by "public order" within the contempl ation of clauses (2), (3) and (4) of article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such murders do not lead to public disorder but to disorder simpliciter.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet, no rational being can say (1)  3 SCR 530.
RatioOfTheDecision	182  that punishment of such murders is not in the general public interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noted that general public interest is not specified as a head in clauses (2) to (4) on which restriction on the rights mentioned in clause (1) of the Article may be justified.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true, as was pointed out by <NAME>, J.
RatioOfTheDecision	(as he then was) in Dr. <NAME>) case, and in several other decisions that followed it, that the real distinction between the areas of 'law and order' and 'public order' lies not merely in the nature or quality of the act, but in the degree and extent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Violent crimes similar in nature, but committed in different contexts and circumstances might cause different reactions.
Precedent	A murder committed in given circumstances may cause only a slight tremor, the wave length of which does not extent beyond the parameters of law and orde r. Another murder committed in different context and circumstances may unleash a tidal wave of such intensity, gravity and magnitude, that its impact throws out of gear the even flow of life.
Precedent	Nonetheless the fact remains that for such murders which do not affect "public order", even the provision for life imprisonment in Section 302 , Indian Penal Code , as as alternative punishment, would not be justifiable under clauses (2), (3) and (4) as a reasonable restriction in the interest of 'Public Order'.
Precedent	Such a construction must, therefore, be avoided.
Precedent	Thus construed, Article 19 will be attracted only to such laws, the provisions of which are capable of being tested under clauses (2) to (5) of Article 19.
Precedent	This proposition was recently (1975) reiterated in <CITATION_1486034> (2).
Precedent	In accord with this line of reasoning in <NAME> case, <ORG> of this Court in <NAME> case restated the principle for the applicability of Article 19 by drawing a distinction between a law of preventive dete ntion and a law providing punishment for commission of crimes, thus :  "Constitution has conferred rights under Article 19 and also adopted preventive detention to prevent the greater evil of elements imperilling the security, the safety of a <ORG> and the welfare of the nation.
Precedent	It is not possible to think that a person who is detained will yet be free to move (1)  1 S.C.R.
Precedent	709.
Precedent	(2) [1975] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	778 at p. 784.
Precedent	183  for assemble or form association or unions or have the right to reside in any part of India or have the freedom of speech or expression.
Precedent	Suppose a person is convicted of an offence of cheating and prosecuted (and imprisoned) after trial, it is not open to say that the imprisonment should be tested with reference to Article 19 for its reasonableness.
Precedent	A law which attracts Article 19 therefore must be such as is capable of being tested to be reasonable under clauses (2) to 5 of Article 19."
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis and parenthesis supplied.)
RatioOfTheDecision	The last sentence which has been underlined by us, appears to lend im plicit approval to the rule of construction adopted by the majority of the learned Judges in <NAME> case, whereby they excluded from the purview of Article 19 certain provisions of the Indian Penal Code providing punishment for certain offences which could not be tested on the specific grounds-embodied in clauses (2) to (5) of that Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	This proposition enunciated in <NAME> case is only a product of the application of the basic canon that a construction which would lead to absurdity, should be eschewed.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <ORG> (popularly known as Bank Nationalization case), the majority adopted the two- fold test for determining as to when a law violated fundamental rights, namely: "(1) It is not the object of the authority making the law impairing the right of a citizen, nor the form of action that determines the protection he can claim.
Precedent	(2) It is the effect of the law and of the action upon the right which attract the jurisdiction of the <ORG> to grant relief.
Precedent	The direct operat ion of the act upon the rights forms the real test."
Precedent	<CITATION_1766147> (ibid), Bhagwati, J. explained the scope of the same test by saying that a law or and order made thereunder will be hit by article 19 , if the direct and inevitable consequence of such law or order is to abridge or take away any one or more of the freedoms guaranteed by Article 19(1).
Precedent	If the effect and operation of the statute by itself, upon a person's fundamental rights is remote or dependent upon "factors which may or may not come into play", then such statute is not ultra-vires on the ground of its being violative of that fundamental right.
Precedent	Bhag-  184  <NAME> described this proposition as "the doctrine of intended and real effect" while <NAME>, J.
Precedent	(as he then was) called it "the test of proximate effect and operation of the statute."
Precedent	The question is, whether <NAME> & <NAME> have given a complete go-by to the 'test of direct and indirect effect, sometimes described as form and object test' or 'pith and substance ru le', which was adopted by <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	and <NAME>, J. in <NAME> case.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, the answer to this question cannot be in the affirmative.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first place, there is nothing much in the name.
RatioOfTheDecision	As <NAME>, J. put it in <NAME> case, such rules of interpretation were evolved only as a matter of reasonableness and common sense and out of the necessity of satisfactorily solving conflicts from the inevitable overlapping of subjects in any distribution of powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the same yardstick of common sense, the 'pith and substance rule' was applied to resolve the question of the constitutionality of a law assailed on the ground of its being violative of a fundamental right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, a survey of the decisions of this <ORG> since <NAME>, shows that the criterion of directness which is the essence of the test of direct and indirect effect, has never been totally abandoned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Only the mode of its application has been modified and its scope amplified by judicial activism to maint ain its efficacy for solving new constitutional problems in tune with evolving concepts of rights and obligations in a strident democracy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The test of direct and indirect effect adopted in <NAME> was approved by <ORG> in <CITATION_959860> .
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) <NAME>, <NAME>, J. quoted with approval the passages (i) and (ii) (which we have extracted earlier) from the judgment of <NAME>, <NAME> Although <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
RatioOfTheDecision	differed on the merits, there was no dissent on this point among all the learned Judges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first decision, which, though purporting to follow <NAME>, <NAME>.
Precedent	enunciation in <NAME>, imperceptibly added another dimension to the test of directness, was <CITATION_1157189> .
Precedent	(3) In that case, the cons-  (1) [1940] FCR 188.
Precedent	(2) [1951] SCR 451.
Precedent	(3) [1959] SCR 12.
Precedent	185  titutional validity of the Working Journalists (Conditions of Service) and Miscellaneous Provisions Act , 1955, and the legality of the decision of <ORG>, constituted thereunder, were challenged.
Precedent	The impug ned Act, which had for its object the regulation of the conditions of service of working journalists and other persons employed in newspaper establishments, provided, inter alia, for the payment of gratuity to a working journalist who had been in continuous service for a certain period.
Precedent	It also regulated hours of work and leave and provided for retrenchment compensation.
Precedent	Section 9 (1) laid down the principles that <ORG> was to follow in fixing the rates of wages of working journalists.
Precedent	One of the contentions of the petitioners in that case was that impugned Act violated their fundamental rights under Articles 19 (1) (a), 19 (1) (g), 14 and 32 of the Constitution and that the decision of <ORG> fixing the rates and scales of wages which imposed too heavy a financial burden on the industry and spelled its total ruin, was illegal and void.
Precedent	It was contended by the learned Attorney General in that case that since the impugned legislation was not a direct legislation on the subject of freedom of s peech and expression.
Precedent	Art.
Precedent	19 (1)(a) would have no application, the test being not the effect or result of legislation but its subject-matter.
Precedent	In support of his contention, he relied upon the observations on this point of Kania, C. J. in <NAME>.
Argument	It was further urged that the object of the impugned Act was only to regulate certain conditions of service of working journalists and other persons employed in the newspaper establishments and not to take away or abridge the freedom of speech or expression enjoyed by the petitioners and, therefore, the impugned Act could not come within the prohibition of Article 19 (1) (a) read with Article 32 of the Constitution.
Argument	On the other hand, the petitioners took their stand on a passage in the decision of <ORG> in Minnesota Ex Rel.
Argument	Olson,(1) which was as under :  "With respect to these contentions it is enough to say that in passing upon constitutional questions the <ORG> has regard to substance and not to mere matters of form, and that,  in accordance with familiar principles, the statute must be tested by its operation and effect."
Argument	(1) [1930] 283 US 697 at p. 708.
Argument	186  It was further submitted that in all such cases, the <ORG> has to look behind the names, forms and appearances to discover the true character and nature of the legislation.
Argument	Thus considered, proceeded the argument, the Act by laying a direct and preferential burden on the press, would tend to curtail the circulation, narrow the scope of dissemination of information and fetter the petitioners' freedom to choose the means of exercising their rights of free speech (which includes the freedom of the press).
Argument	It was further submitted that those newspaper employers who were marginally situated may not be able to bear the strain and have to disappear after closing down their establishments.
Argument	<NAME>, J. who delivered the unanimous Judgment of <ORG>, after noting that the object of the impugned legislation is to provide for the amelioration of the conditions of  the workmen in the newspaper industry, overruled this contention of the employers, thus:  "That, however would be a consequence which would be extraneous and not within the contemplation of the legislature.
RatioOfTheDecision	It could therefore hardly be urged that the possible effect of the impact of these measures in conceivable cases would vitiate the legislation as such.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the consequences which have been visualized in the behalf by the petitioners, viz., the tendency to curtail circulation and thereby narrow the scope of dissemination of information, fetters on the petitioners' freedom to choose the means of exercising the right, likelihood of the independence of the press being undermined by having to seek government aid; the imposition of penalty on the petitioners' right to choose the instruments for exercising the freedom or compelling them to seek alternative media, etc., would be remote and depend upon various factors which may or may not come into play.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unless these were the direct or inevitable consequences of th e measures enacted in the impugned Act, it would not be possible to strike down the legislation as having that effect and operation."
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis added) The learned Judge further observed that the impugned Act could be "legitimately characterised as a measure which affects the press", but its "intention or the proximate effect and operation" was not such as would take away or abridge the right of freedom of speech and expression guaranteed in Article 19 (1) (a ), therefore, it could not be held invalid on that ground.
RatioOfTheDecision	The impugned decision of <ORG>, however, was held to be ultra vires the Act and contrary to the principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be observed at this place that the manner in which the test of direct and indirect effect was applied by <NAME>, was not very different from the mode in which <NAME> applied it to punitive detention as punishment after conviction for an offence under the Indian Penal Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, did not discard the test adopted by <NAME>, C. J., in <NAME>, in its entirety; he merely extended the application of the criterion of directness to the operation and effect of the impugned legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again, in <CITATION_243002> (1) this <ORG>, while considering the constitutional validity of the Newspaper (Price and Page) Act , 1956 and Daily Newspaper (Price and Page) Order, 1960, held that the "direct and immediate" effect of the impugned Order would be to restrain a newspaper from publishing any number of pages for carrying its news and views, which it has a fundamental right under Article 19 (1) (a) and, therefore, the Order was violative of the right of the newspapers guaranteed by Article 19 (1) (a ), and as such, invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case, also, the emphasis had shifted from the object and subject- matter of the impugned State action to its direct and immediate effect.
Argument	<CITATION_1643138> .,(2) an order prohibiting the publication of the evidence of a witness in a defamation case, passed by a learned Judge (<NAME>, J.)
Argument	of <ORG> urt, was impugned on the ground that it violated the petitioners' right to free speech and expression guaranteed by Article 19 (1) (a).
Argument	<NAME>, C.J., (<NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, <ORG>., concurring) repelled this contention with these illuminating observations:  "The argument that the impugned order affects the fundamental rights of the petitioners under Article 19 (1 ), is based on a complete misconception about the true nature and (1)  3 SCR 842.
Precedent	(2) [1966] 3 SCR 744.
Precedent	188  character of judicial process and of judicial decisions.
Precedent	When a Judge deals with matters brought before him for his adjudication, he first decides questions of fact on which the parties are at issue, and then applies the relevant law to the said facts.
Precedent	Whether the findings of fact recorded by the Judge are right or wrong, and whether the conclusion of law drawn by him suffers from any infirmity, can be considered and decided if the party aggrieved by the decision of the Judge takes the matter up before the appellate Co urt.
Precedent	But it is singularly inappropriate to assume that a judicial decision pronounced by a Judge of competent jurisdiction in or in relation to matter brought before him for adjudication can affect the fundamental rights of the citizens under Article 19 (1).
Precedent	What the judicial decision purports to do is to decide the controversy between the parties brought before the court and nothing more.
Precedent	If this basic and essential aspect of the judicial process is borne in mind, it would be plain that the judicial verdict pronounced by court in or in relation to a matter brought before it for its decision cannot be said to affect the fundamental rights of citizens under Article 19 (1)."
Precedent	"It is well-settled that in examining the validity of legislation, it is legitimate to consider whether the impugned legislation is a legislation directly in respect of the subject covered by any particular article of the Constitution, or touches the said article only incidentally or indirectly'.'
Precedent	"If the test of direct effect and object w hich is sometimes described as the pith and substance test, is thus applied in considering the validity of legislation, it would not be inappropriate to apply the same test to judicial decisions like the one with which we are concerned in the present proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	As we have already indicated, the impugned order was directly concerned with giving such protection to the witness as was thought to be necessary in order to obtain true evidence in the case with a view to do justice between the parties.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, incidentally, as a result of this-order, the petitioners were not able to report what they heard in court, that cannot be said to make the impugned order invalid under Article 19 (1) (a)."
RatioOfTheDecision	189  We have already mentioned briefly how the test of directness was developed and reached its culmination in <ORG> case and <NAME> case.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the above conspectus, it is clear that the test of direct and indirect effect was not scrapped.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, there is no dispute that the test of 'pith and subst ance' of the subject-matter and of direct and of incidental effect of legislation is a very useful test to determine the question of legislative competence i.e., in ascertaining whether an Act falls under one Entry while incidentally encroaching upon another Entry.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even for determining the validity of a legislation on the ground of infringement of fundamental rights, the subject-matter and the object of the legislation are not altogether irrelevant.
Precedent	For instance, if the subject- matter of the legilation directly covers any of the fundamental freedoms mentioned in Article 19 (1 ), it must pass the test of reasonableness under the relevant head in clauses (2) to (6) of that Article.
Precedent	If the legislation does not directly deal with any of the rights in Article 19 (1 ), that may not conclude the enquiry.
Precedent	It will have to be ascertained further whether by its direct and immediate operation, the impugned legislation abridges any of the rights enumerated in Article 19 (1).
Precedent	In <NAME>) <NAME>, J. in his dis senting judgment referred with approval to the test as expounded in <ORG>.
Precedent	He further observed that "the 'pith and substance' test, though not strictly appropriate, must serve a useful purpose in the process of deciding whether the provisions in question which work some interference with the freedom of speech, are essentially regulatory in character".
Precedent	From a survey of the cases noticed above, a comprehensive test which can be formulated, may be re- stated as under:  Does the impugned law, in its pith and substance, whatever may be its form and object, deal with any of the fundamental rights conferred by Article 19 (1)?
Precedent	If it does, does it abridge or abrogate any of those rights?
Precedent	And even if it does not, in its pith and substance, deal with any of the fundamental rights conferred by Article 19(1 ), is the Direct and inevitable effect of the impugned law such as to abridge or abrogate any of those rights?
Precedent	The mere fact that the impugned law incidentally, remotely or collaterally has the effect of a bridging or abrogating those rights, will not satisfy the test.
Precedent	If the answer to the above queries be in the affirmative, the impugned law in order to be valid, must pass the test of reasonableness under Article 19.
Precedent	But if the impact of the law on any of the rights under clause (1) of Article 19 is merely incidental, indirect, remote or collateral and is dependent upon factors which may or may not come into play, the anvil of Article 19 will not be avilable for judging its validity.
Precedent	Now, let us apply this test to the provisions of the Penal Code in question.
Precedent	Section 299 defines 'culpable homicide' and Section 300 defines culpable homicide amounting to murder.
Precedent	Section 302 prescribes death or imprisonment for life as penalty for murder.
Precedent	It cannot, reasonably or rationally, be contended that any of the rights mentioned in Article 19(1) of the Constitution confers the freedom to commit murder or, for the matter of that, the freedom to commit any offence whatsoever.
Precedent	Therefore, penal laws, that is to say, laws whi ch define offences and prescribe punishment for the commission of offences do not attract the application of Article 19(1).
Precedent	We cannot, of course, say that the object of penal laws is generally such as not to involve any violation of the rights conferred by Article 19(1) because after the decision of this <ORG> in the Bank Nationalization case the theory, that the object and form of the <ORG> action alone determine the extent of protection that may be claimed by an individual and that the effect of the <ORG> action on the fundamental right of the individual is irrelevant, stands discredited.
Precedent	But the point of the matter is that, in pith and substance, penal laws do not deal with the subject matter of rights enshrined in Article 19(1).
Precedent	That again is not enough for the purpose of deciding upon the applicability of Article 19 because as the test formulated by us above shows, even if a law does not, in its pith and substance, deal with any of the fundamental rights conferred by Article 19(1 ), if the direct and ine vitable effect of the law is such as to abridge or abrogate any of those rights, Article 19(1) shall have been attracted.
Precedent	It would then become necessary to test the validity of even a penal law on the touchstone of that Article.
Precedent	On this latter aspect of the matter, we are of the opinion that the deprivation of freedom consequent upon an order of conviction and sentence is not a direct and inevitable consequence of the penal law but is merely incidental to the order of conviction and sentence which may or may not come into play, that is to say, which may or may not be passed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Considering therefore the test formulated by us in its dual aspect, we are of the opinion that Section 302 of the Penal Code does not have to stand the test of Article 19(1) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is particularly true of crimes, inherently vicious and pernicious, which under the English Common Law were classified as crimes <NAME> in se as distinguished from crimes <NAME> prohibita crimes <NAME> in se embrace acts immoral or wrong in themsel ves, such as, murder, rape, arson, burglary, larceny (robbery and dacoity); while crimes <NAME> prohibita embrace things prohibited by statute as infringing on others' rights, though no moral turpitude attaches to such crimes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such acts constitute crimes only because they are so prohibited.
Precedent	(See Words and Phrases, Permanent Edition, Vol.
Precedent	10).
Precedent	While crimes <NAME> in se do not per se, or in operation directly and inevitably impinge on the rights under Article 19(1 ), cases under the other category of crimes are conceivable where the law relating to them directly restricts or abridges such rights.
Precedent	The illustration given by <NAME> will make the point clear.
Precedent	Suppose, a law is enacted which provides that it shall be an offence to level any criticism, whatever, of the Government established by law and makes a further provision prescribing five years' imprisonment as punishment for such an offence.
Precedent	Such a law (i.e.
Precedent	its provision defining the offence) will directly and inevitably impinge upon the right guaranteed u nder clause  (a) of Article 19(1).
Precedent	Therefore, to be valid, it must pass the test of reasonableness embodied in clause (2) of the Article.
Precedent	But this cannot be said in regard to the provisions of the Penal Code with which we are concerned.
Precedent	Assuming arguendo, that the provisions of the Penal Code , particularly those providing death penalty as an alternative punishment for murder, have to satisfy the requirements of reasonableness and public interest under Article 19 the golden strand of which according to the ratios of <NAME> runs through the basic structure of Article 21 also the further questions to be determined, in this connection, will be: On whom will the onus of satisfying the requirements under Article 19 , lie ?
RatioOfTheDecision	Will such onus lie on the <ORG> or the person challenging its validity ?
RatioOfTheDecision	And what will be the nature of the onus?
RatioOfTheDecision	192  With regard to onus, no hard and fast rule of universal application in all situations, can be deducted from the decided cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	In some decisions, such as, <CITATION_114 8508> (1) and <CITATION_668225> .
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) it was laid down by this <ORG> that if the writ petitioner succeeds in showing that the impugned law ex facie abridges or transgresses the rights coming under any of the sub-clauses of clause (1) of Article 19 , the onus shifts on the respondent state to show that the legislation comes within the permissible limits imposed by any of the clauses (2) to (6) as may be applicable to the case, and, also to place material before the court in support of that contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the <ORG> does nothing in that respect, it is not for the petitioner to prove negatively that it is not covered by any of the permissive clauses.
RatioOfTheDecision	A contrary trend, however, is discernible in the recent decisions of this <ORG>, which start with the initial presumption in favour of the constitutionality of the statute and throw the burden of rebutting that presumption on the party who challenges its constitutionality on the ground of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_843614> (3) this <ORG>, speaking through <NAME> er, J., reiterated the ratio of <NAME> case,(4) that :  "there is always a presumption in favour of the constitutionality of an enactment and the burden is upon him who attack it to show that there has been a clear transgression of the constitutional principles"; and "that it must be presumed that the legislature understands and correctly appreciates the need of its own people, that its laws are directed to problems made manifest by experience and that its discriminations are based on adequate grounds."
RatioOfTheDecision	It was emphasised that "Judges act not by hunch but on hard facts properly brought on record and sufficiently strong to rebuff the initial presumption of constitutionality of legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor is the <ORG> a third Chamber of the <ORG> to weigh whether it should draft the clause differently".
Precedent	Referring, inter alia, to the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> (ibid), and <NAME>'s 'Constitutional Law of India', Vol.
Precedent	I, page 54, it was recalled, "Some courts have gone to the extent of hol ding that there is a presumption in favour of constitutionality, and a law will not be declared unconstitutional unless the case is so clear as to be free from doubt; and to doubt the constitutionality of a law is to resolve it in favour of its validity".
Precedent	Similar view was taken by a Bench of seven learned Judges of this Court in <CITATION_1959928> .
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) Behind the view that there is a presumption of constitutionality of a statute and the onus to rebut the same lies on those who challenge the legislation, is the rationale of judicial restraint, a recognition of the limits of judicial review; a respect for the boundaries of legislative and judicial functions, and the judicial responsibility to guard the trespass from one side or the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The primary function of the courts is to interpret and apply the laws according to the will of those who made them and not to transgress into the legislative domain of policy-making.
Precedent	"The job of a Judge is judging and not law- making".
Precedent	In Lord <NAME>'s words : "Judges are the  keepers of the law and the keepers of these boundaries cannot, also, be among out-riders."
Precedent	A similar warning was echoed by <ORG> in <NAME> v. United States(2) in these terms :  "Courts are not representative bodies.
Precedent	They are not designed to be a good reflex of a democratic society.
Precedent	Their judgment is best informed, and therefore most dependable, within narrow limits.
Precedent	Their essential quality is detachment, founded on independence.
Precedent	History teaches that the independence of the judiciary is jeopardized when courts become embroiled in the passions of the day and assume primary responsibility in choosing between competing political, economic and social pressures."
Precedent	194  In <NAME> v. Georgia,(1) one of the principal questions for consideration was, whether capital punishment provided in a statute for certain crimes was a "cruel and unusual" punishment.
Precedent	In that context, the nature of the burden which rests on those who attack the constitutionality of the statute was explained by <NAME> t, J., thus :  "We may not require the legislature to select the least severe penalty possible so long as the penalty selected is not cruelly inhumane or disproportionate to the crime involved.
Precedent	And a heavy burden rests on those who would attack the judgment of the representatives of the people.
Precedent	This is true in part because the constitutional test is intertwined with an assessment of contemporary standards and the legislative judgment weighs heavily in ascertaining such standards.
Precedent	In a democratic society legislatures, not courts, are constituted to respond to the will and conse quently the moral values of the people."
Precedent	Even where the burden is on the <ORG> to show that the restriction imposed by the impugned statute is reasonable and in public interest, the extent and the manner of discharge of the burden necessarily depends on the subject- matter of the legislation, the nature of the inquiry, and the scope and limits of judicial review.
Precedent	(See the observations of <NAME> in <CITATION_1409304> ,(2) reiterated  in <NAME>).
Precedent	In the instant case, the <ORG> has discharged its burden primarily by producing for the persual of the <ORG>, the 35th Report of <ORG>, 1967, and the judgments of this <ORG> in <NAME> Singh and in several subsequent cases, in which it has been recognised that death penalty serves as a deterrent.
Precedent	It is, therefore, for the petitioners to prove and establish that the death sentence for murder is so outmoded, unusual or excessive as to be devoid of any rational nexus with the purpose and object of the legislation.
Precedent	<ORG>, after making an intensive and extensive study of the subject of death penalty in India, published and submitted its 35th Report in 1967 to the Government.
Precedent	After examining, a wealth of evidential material and considering the arguments for and against its retention, that high-powered Body summed up its conclusions at page 354 of its Report, as follows :  "The issue of abolition or retention has to be decided on a balancing of the various argumen ts for and against retention.
Precedent	No single argument for abolition or retention can decide the issue.
Precedent	In arriving at any conclusion on the subject, the need for protecting society in general and individual human beings must be borne in mind.
Precedent	It is difficult to rule out the validity of, of the strength behind, many of the arguments for abolition nor does, the <ORG> treat lightly the argument based on the irrevocability of the sentence of death, the need for a modern approach, the severity of capital punishment and the strong feeling shown by certain sections of public opinion in stressing deeper questions of human values.
Precedent	Having regard, however, to the conditions in India, to the variety of the social up-bringing of its inhabitants, to the disparity in the level of morality and education in the country, to the vastness of its area, to diversity of its population and to the paramount need for maintaining law and order in the country at the present juncture, India cannot risk the experiment of abolition of ca pital punishment."
Precedent	This Report was also, considered by the Constitution Bench of this <ORG> in Jagmohan.
Precedent	It was the main piece of evidence on the basis of which the challenge to the constitutional validity of Section 302 of the Penal Code, on the ground of its being violative of Article 19 , was repelled.
Precedent	<ORG> must be presumed to have considered these views of <ORG> and the judgment of this <ORG> in Jagmohan, and must also have been aware of the principles crystallised by judicial precedents in the matter of sentencing when it took up revision of the Code of Criminal Procedure in 1973, and inserted in it, Section 354(3) which indicates that death penalty can be awarded in exceptional cases for murder and for some other offences under the Penal Code for special reasons to be recorded.
Precedent	Death penalty has been the subject of an age-old debate between Abolitionists and Retentionists, although recently the controversy has come in sharp focus.
Precedent	Both the groups are deeply anchored in their antagon istic views.
Precedent	Both firmly and sincerly believe in the rightcousness of their respective stands, with overtones of sentiment and emotion.
Precedent	Both the camps can claim among them eminent thinkers, penologists, sociologists, jurists; judges, legislators, administrators and law enforcement officials.
Precedent	The chief arguments of the Abolitionists, which have been substantially adopted by the learned counsel for the petitioners, are as under :  (a) The death penalty is irreversible.
Precedent	Decided upon according to fallible processes of law by fallible human beings, it can be-and actually has been- inflicted upon people innocent of any crime.
Precedent	(b) There is no convincing evidence to show that death penalty serves any penological purpose :  (i) Its deterrent effect remains unproven.
Precedent	It has not been shown that incidence of murder has increased in countries where death penalty has been abolished, after its abolition.
Precedent	(ii) Retribution in the sense of vengeance, is no longer an acceptable end of punishment.
Precedent	(iii)On the contrary, refor mation of the criminal and his rehabilitation is the primary purpose of punishment.
Precedent	Imposition of death penalty nullifies that purpose.
Precedent	(c) Execution by whatever means and for whatever offence is a cruel, inhuman and degrading punishment.
Precedent	It is proposed to deal with these arguments, as far as possible, in their serial order.
Precedent	Regarding (a) : It is true that death penalty is irrevocable and a few instances, can be cited, including some from England, of persons who after their conviction and execution for murder, were discovered to be innocent.
Precedent	But this, according to the Retentionists is not a reason for abolition of the death penalty, but an argument for reform of the judicial system and the sentencing procedure.
Precedent	Theore-  197  tically, such errors of judgment cannot be absolutely eliminated from any system of justice, devised and worked by human beings, but their incidence can be infinitesimally reduced by providing adequate safeguards and checks.
Precedent	We will presently see, while dealing with the procedural aspe ct of the problem, that in India, ample safeguards have been provided by law and the Constitution which almost eliminate the chances of an innocent person being convicted and executed for a capital offence.
Precedent	Regarding (b): Whether death penalty serves any penological purpose.
Precedent	Firstly, in most of the countries in the world, including India, a very large segment of the population, including notable penologists judges, jurists, legislators and other enlightened people still believe that death penalty for murder and certain other capital offences does serve as a deterrent, and a greater deterrent than life imprisonment.
Precedent	We will set out very briefly, by way of sample, opinions of some of these distinguished persons.
Precedent	In the first place, we will notice a few decisions of Courts wherein the deterrent value of death penalty has been judicially recognised.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. State of Punjab,(1) the facts were that <NAME>, who was a fanatic devotee of the <NAME>, used to hold Satsangs at which bhajans were sung in prai se of the Goddess.
Precedent	<NAME> ceremonially beheaded his four year old boy at the crescendo of the morning bhajan.
Precedent	He was tried, convicted and sentenced to death for the murder.
Precedent	His death sentence was confirmed by <ORG>.
Precedent	He filed a petition for grant of special leave to appeal to this Court under Article 136 of the Constitution.
Precedent	It was contended on behalf of <NAME> that the very monstrosity of the crime provided proof of his insanity sufficient to exculpate the offender under Section 84 , Indian Penal Code , or material for mitigation of the sentence of death.
Precedent	<NAME>, J., speaking for the Bench, to which one of us (Sarkaria, J.)

RatioOfTheDecision	The shortcoming is basic, because the implications following from the general deterrence hypothesis are what <NAME> was challenging.
