RatioOfTheDecision	... ... ...  "Another fundamental shortcoming of <NAME>'s studies is their failure to account systematically for other factors that are expected by the deterrence hypothesis to affect the frequency of murder in the population, apart from the relevant risk of execution.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are variables such as the probability of apprehension, the conditional probability of conviction given apprehension, the severity of alternative punishments for murder, the distribution of income, the probability of unemployment, and other indicators of differential gains from criminal activities occurring jointly with murder.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since, as I shall argue later, some of these variables are expected to be highly correlated with the conditional probability of execution given conviction of murder, their exclusion from the statistical analysis can seriously bias estimates of the partial deterrent effec t of capital punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Aware of the problem, <NAME> attempted to compare states that are as alike as possible in all other respects.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, his "matching procedure", based on the assumption that neighbouring states can satisfy such pre-requisites without any explicit standardization, is simply insufficient for any valid inferences.
Fact	Pairs of states, such as New York, and Rhode Island, Massachusetts and Maine, or Illinois and Wisconsin all included in his comparisons, differ in their economic and demographic characteristics, in their law enforcement activities, and in the opportunities they provide for the commission of other crimes.
Fact	Moreover, the direction of the causal relationship between the murder rate and the overall risk of punishment-be it the death penalty or any other sanction - is not self-evident because, for example, states with high murder rates are expected to and, in fact do devote more resources to apprehend, convict and execute offenders than do states with lower rates.
Precedent	Specifically, varia tions in the legal or practical status of the death penalty occasionally may be the result of, rather than the cause for, changes in the murder rate, and thus may give rise to an apparent positive association between these two variables.
Precedent	The same general point applies in connection with the identification of the effect of any other variable which is a product of law enforcement activity or private protection against crime.
Precedent	For these reasons, the true deterrent effect of a sanction such as the death penalty cannot be readily inferred from simple comparisons of the sort performed by <NAME>."

Precedent	<ORG> in its 35th Report, after carefully sifting all the materials collected by them, recorded their views regarding the deterrent effect of capital punishment as follows:  "In our view capital punishment does act as a deterrent.
Precedent	We have already discussed in detail several aspects o f this topic.
Precedent	We state below, very briefly, the main points that have weighed with us in arriving at this conclusion:  (a) Basically, every human being dreads death.
Precedent	(b) Death, as a penalty, stands on a totally different level from imprisonment for life or any otber punishment.
Precedent	The difference is one of quality, and not merely of degree.

Precedent	Views of <ORG>:  <ORG>, after making an exhaustive study of the issue of capital punishment and its deterrent value, in their Report (1949-53), concluded:  "The general conclusion which we reach, after careful review of all the evidence we have been able to obtain as to the deterrent effect of capital punishment, may be stated as follows.
Precedent	Prima facie the penalty of death is likely to have a stronger effect as a deterrent to normal human beings than any other form of punishment, and there is some evidence (though no convincing statistical evidence) that this is in fact so.
Precedent	But this effect does not operate universally or uniformly, and there are many offenders on whom it is limited and may often be negligible."
Precedent	We may add that whether or not death penalty in actual practice acts as a de terrent, cannot be statistically proved, either way, because statistics as to how many potentisim murderers were deterred from committing murders, but for the existence of capital punishment for murder, are difficult, if not altogether impossible, to collect.
Precedent	Such statistics of deterred potential murderers are difficult to unravel as they remain hidden in the innermost recesses of their mind.
Precedent	Retribution in the sense of reprobation whether a totally rejected concept of punishment.
Precedent	Even retribution in the sense of society's reprobation for the worst of crimes, i.e., murder, is not an altogether outmoded concept.
Precedent	This view is held by many distinguished sociologist, jurists and judges.
Precedent	Lord Justice <NAME>, Master of the Rolls of the Court of Appeal in England, appearing before <ORG>, stated his views on this point as under:  "Punishment is the way in which society expresses its denunciation of wrong-doing, and, in order to maintain respect for law, it is essential  that the punishment inflicted for grave crimes should adequately reflect the revulsion felt by the great majority of citizens for them.
Precedent	It is a mistake to consider the objects of punishment as being deterrent or reformative or preventive and nothing else...The truth is that some crimes are so outrageous that society insists on adequate punishment, because the wrong-doer deserves it, irrespective of whether it is a deterrent or not."
Precedent	That retribution is still socially acceptable function of punishment, was also the view expressed by <NAME>, J., in <NAME> v. Georgia, at page 389, as follows:  "...I would say only that I cannot agree that retribution is a constitutionally impermissible ingredient in the imposition of punishment.
Precedent	The instinct for retribution is part of the nature of man, and channeling that instant, in the administration of criminal justice serves an important purpose in promoting the stability of a society governed by law.
Precedent	When people begin to believe that organized society is unwilling or un able to impose upon criminal offenders the punishment they 'deserve', then there are sown the seeds of anarchy of self help, vigilant justice, and lynch law."

Precedent	Vindictiveness is the emotional outflow of retribution and justice has no concern with that.

RatioOfTheDecision	Retribution and deterrence are not two divergent ends of capital punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are convergent goals which ultimately merge into one.
RatioOfTheDecision	How these ends of punishment coalesce into one was described by <ORG>, thus:  "The retributive object of capital punishment has been the subject-matter of sharp attack at the hands of the abolitionists.

RatioOfTheDecision	How far it should form part of the penal philosophy in modern times will always remain a matter of controversy.
RatioOfTheDecision	No useful purpose will be served by a discussion as to whether the instinct of retribution is or is not commendable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fact remains, however, that whenever there is a serious crime, the society feels a sense of disapprobation.

Precedent	The law does not encourage it, or exploit it for any undesirable ends.
Precedent	Rather, by reserving the death penalty for murder, and thus visiting this gravest crime with the gravest punishment, the law helps the element of retribution merge into the element of deterrence."
Precedent	[Para 265 (18), 35th Report] Earlier in 1949-1953, <ORG> in Para 59 of its Report spoke in a somewhat similar strain:  "We think it is reasonable to suppose that the deterrent force of capital punishment operates not only by affecting the conscious thoughts of individuals tempted to commit murder, but also by building up in the community, over a long period of time, a deep feeling of peculiar abhorrence for the crime of murder.

Precedent	The argument cannot be rejected out of hand.
Precedent	A number of instances can be cited where abolitionist States feeling the inadequacy of their penological armour to combat politically motivated gangsterism, have retrieved and used their capital weapon which they had once thrown away.
Precedent	Despite their traditional abhorrence of death penalty, the Norwegians executed Major <NAME> afte r World War II.
Precedent	The Belgians, too, executed no less than 242 collaborators' and traitors after the liberation, although in their country, the death penalty was otiose since 1880.
Precedent	In England, death penalty was retained for high treason in the Silverman Bill of 1956.
Precedent	Even at present, for that offence, death penalty is a valid sanction in England.
Precedent	In the aftermath of assassination of Prime Minister <NAME> in 1959, Ceylon hurriedly reintroduced capital punishment for murder.
Precedent	Owing to similar considerations, Israel sanctioned death penalty for crimes committed against the Jewish people, and executed the notorious Jew-baiter, <NAME> in 1962.
Precedent	Recently, on April 9, 1979, confronted with a wave of violent incidents after the signing of Egypt- Israel Peace Treaty.
Precedent	Israel sanctioned the use of death penalty "for acts of inhuman cruelty".
Precedent	____________ (1) Sunday Tribune, December 8, 1963.
Precedent	215  In India, very few scientific studies in regard to crime and punishment in general, and capital punishment, in pa rticular, have been made.
Precedent	Counsel for the petitioners referred us to Chapter VI, captioned 'Capital Punishment, in the book, 'Quantum of Punishment in Criminal Law in India, written by Dr. <NAME>, now on the staff of <ORG>, Amritsar.
Precedent	In this article, which was primarily meant as LL.
Precedent	D. thesis, the learned author concludes:  "On the basis of statistics both of India and abroad, <ORG> findings and other weighty arguments, we can safely conclude that death penalty is not sustainable on merits.
Precedent	Innately it has no reformative element.
Precedent	It has been proved that death penalty as operative carries no deterrent value and crime of murder is governed by factors other than death penalty.
Precedent	Accordingly, I feel that the death penalty should be abolished."
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be seen, in the first place, that the analysis by Dr. <NAME> in coming to the conclusion, that death penalty is of no penological value, is based on stale, incomplete and inadequate statistics.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is more particularly true of the data rela ting to India, which does not cover the period subsequent to 1961.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, the approach to the problem adopted by him, like the other Abolitionists referred to by him, is mainly, if not merely, statistical.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already noticed, the proponents of the opposite view of capital punishment, point out that statistics alone are not determinative of the question whether or not death penalty serves any deterent or other penological purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, statistics of deterred potential murderers are hard to obtain.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, the approach adopted by the Abolitionists is oversimplified at the cost of other relevant but imponderable factors, the appreciation of which is essential to assess the true penological value of capital punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The number of such factors is infinitude, their character variable duration transient and abstract formulation difficult.
RatioOfTheDecision	Conditions change from country to country and time to time.
RatioOfTheDecision	Due to the inconstancy of social conditions, it is not scientifically possible to assess with any degree of  accuracy, as to whether the variation in the incidence of capital crime is attributable to the presence or absence of death penalty in the penal law of that country for such crimes.
RatioOfTheDecision	216  That is why statistical attempts to assess the true penological value of capital punishment, remain inconclusive.
RatioOfTheDecision	Pursued beyond a certain point, both the Abolitionists and the Retentionists retreat into their own conceptual bunkers firmly entrenched in their respective "faiths".
RatioOfTheDecision	We need not take sides with either of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is always a danger in adhering too rigidly to concepts.
RatioOfTheDecision	As Prof. <NAME> has pointed out "all concepts are abstractions from reality, and that in the process of abstraction something of the reality is bound to be lost''(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	We must therefore, view the problem against the perspective of the hard realities of the time and the conditions prevailing in the world, particularly in our own country.
RatioOfTheDecision	A review of the world events of the last seven or eight years, as evident from <ORG>  Books and other material referred to by the learned counsel, would show that most countries in the world are in the grip of an ever-rising tide of violent crime.
Fact	Murders for monetary gain or from misdirected political motives, robbery, rape assault are on the increase.
Fact	India is no exception.
Fact	<ORG> has produced for our perusal a statement of facts and figures showing the incidence of violent crime, including murder, dacoity and robbery, in the various States of India, during the years 1965 to 1975.
Fact	Another statement has been furnished showing the number of persons convicted of murder and other capital offences and sentenced to death in some of the States of India during the period 1974 to 1978.
RatioOfTheDecision	This statement however, is incomplete and inadequate.
RatioOfTheDecision	On account of that deficiency and for the general reasons set out above, it cannot, even statistically show conclusively or with any degree of certainty, that capital punishment has no penological worth.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the first statement does bring out clearly the  stark reality that the crimes of murder, dacoity and robbery in India are since 1965 increasing.
Precedent	Now, looking around at the world during the last decade, we may recall that in <NAME> v. Georgia (decided on June 29, 1976), <ORG> of the United States held by a majority, that the imposition and carrying out of the death penalty constitutes 'cruel and unusual' punishment, in violation of the Eighth and Fourteenth ____________ (1) An Enquiry into Criminal Guilt by Prof. <NAME>, 1963 Edn.
Precedent	Melbourne, page 13.
Precedent	217  Amendments.
Precedent	<NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	(differing from the plurality) went to the extent of holding that death penalty was per se unconstitutional as it was a cruel and unusual punishment.
Precedent	In so holding, these learned Justices purported to adopt the contemporary standards of decency prevailing among the enlightened public of the United States.
Precedent	Justice <NAME> ruled that "it was morally unacceptable to the people of the United States".
RatioOfTheDecision	This opinion of the learned Justices was sharply reb uffed by the people of the United States through their chosen representatives.
RatioOfTheDecision	Soon after the decision in Furman, bowing to the thrust of public opinion, the <ORG> of not less than 32 States, post-haste revised their penal laws and reinstituted death penalty for murder and certain other crimes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Public opinion polls then taken show that approximately 70 per cent of Americans have been in favour of death penalty.
RatioOfTheDecision	(See 'The Voice', supra).
RatioOfTheDecision	In 1976, a <ORG> Poll taken in the Unitted States showed that more than 65 per cent of those polled preferred to have an operative death penalty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Incidently, the rejection by the people of the approach adopted by the two learned Judges in Furman, furnishes proof of the fact that judicial opinion does not necessarily reflect the moral attitudes of the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, it is a reminder that Judges should not take upon themselves the responsibility of becoming oracles or spokesmen of public opinion: Not being representatives of the people, it is often better, as  a matter of judicial restraint, to leave the function of assessing public opinion to the chosen representatives of the people in the legislature concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Coming back to the review of the world crime situation, during the last decade, Saudi Arabia and some other countries have reinstated death penalty or enacted harsher punishments not only for murder but some other crimes, also.
RatioOfTheDecision	In America, apart from 32 States which reinstated death penalty under revised laws after <NAME>, the legislatures of some of the remaining 15 States have either reinstituted or are considering to reintroduce death penalty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Currently, a federal legislation for reinstituting or prescribing capital punishment for a larger range of offences of homicide is under consideration of United States' <ORG>.
Fact	According to the report of <ORG>, in U.S.A., as on May 1, 1979, death penalty can be imposed for aggravated murder in 35 States.
Fact	Attempts have been made in other countries, also to reintroduce death penalty.
Fact	In Brita in, in the wake of serious violent incidents of terrorism, a Bill was moved in <ORG> to reintroduce capital punishment for murder and certain other offences.
Precedent	It was defeated by a free vote on April 19, 1979.
Precedent	Even so, no less than 243 Members of <ORG> had voted in favour of this measure.
Precedent	We have noted that Israel has also recently reinstituted death penalty for certain criminal 'acts of in human cruelty'.
Precedent	In <ORG>, a new legislation was adopted on July 1, 1979 by China's <ORG>, according to Article 43 of which, death penalty can be imposed "for the most heinous crimes".
Precedent	In Argentina, the death penalty was reintroduced in 1976.
Precedent	Similarly, Belgium reintroduced death penalty and increased the number of crimes punishable with death.
Precedent	In France, in 1978 a movement in favour of abolition initiated by the French bishops failed to change the law under which death penalty is a valid sanction for murder and certain other offences.
Precedent	In Japan, death penalty is a legal sanction for 13 crim es.
Precedent	In Greece and Turkey, death penalty can be imposed for murder and other capital offences.
Precedent	In Malaysia and the Republic of Singapore under the Drugs Act of May, 1979, misuse of drugs is also punishable with death.
Precedent	Cuba introduced a new penal code in February 1978, which provides punishment of death by shooting for crimes ranging from some types of murder and robbery to hijacking and rape.
Precedent	In the U.S.S.R. (Russia), as many as 18 offences are punishable with death.
Fact	In Russia, at present, the following offences committed in peacetime are punishable with death under the RSFSR Criminal Code:  "Treason ( Article 64 ); espoinage ( Article 65 ); terrorism (if the offence includes the killing of an official ( Article 66 ); terrorism against representative of foreign <ORG> (if the offence includes the killing of such a representative "for the purpose of provoking war or international compli cations") ( Article 67 ); sabotage ( Article 68 ); organizing the commission of any of the above-named offences ( Article 72 ) ; commission of any of the above-named offences against other <ORG> ( Article 73 ); banditry ( Article 77 ); actions disrupting the work of corrective labour institutions ( Article 77-1 ); making or passing counterfeit money or securities (when the offence is committed as a form of business) ( Article 87 ); violation of rules for currency transactions (when committed as a form of business or on a large scale, or by a person previously convicted under this Article) ( Art.
Fact	88 ); stealing of <ORG> property on an especially large scale, regardless of the manner of stealing ( Article 93-1 ); intentional homicide with aggravating circumstances ( Article 102 ); rape, when committed by a group of persons or by an especially dangerous recidivist, or resulting in especially grave consequences, or the rape of a minor ( Article 117 ); taking a bribe, with especially aggravating circumstances ( Article 173 ); infringing the life of a policeman or <ORG>, with aggravating circumstances ( Article 19 1-2 ); hijacking an aircraft, if the offence results in death or serious physical injuries ( Article 213-2 ); resisting a superior or compelling him to violate official duties, an offence applicable only to military personnel, and carrying the death penalty in peace-time if committed in conjunction with intentional homicide of a superior or any other person performing military duties ( Article 240 )."
Fact	(<ORG>, <ORG>, 1979) Our object in making the above survey is to bring out the hard fact that in spite of the Abolitionist movement, only 18 States (as on 30 May 1979) in the world have abolished the death penalty for all offences, while 8 more have retained it for specific offences committed in time of war, only.
Fact	(See Amnesty International Report (1979) page 92).
Fact	This means, most of the countries in the modern world still retain death penalty as a legal sanction for certain specified offences.
Fact	The countries which retain death penalty in their penal laws, such as, Russia, U.S.A., Franc e, Belgium, Malaysia, China and Japan, etc., cannot, by any standard, be called uncivilized nations or immature societies.
Fact	Surveyors and students of world events and current trends believe that the reversal of the attitudes towards criminals and their judicial punishments in general, and capital punishment in particular in several countries of the world, is partly due to the fact that milder sanctions or corrective processes, or even the alternative of imprisonment, have been found inadequate and wanting to stem the mounting tide of serious crime.
Fact	Writing in <ORG>, 1978 Book of the Year under the caption, 'Changing Attitudes Towards Criminals', <NAME> sums up the cause that has led to the adoption of this New Hard Line, thus :  220  "Horror Story after horror story of dangerous criminals sent back into society on bail or parole from a penitentiary or (in many cases) release from a mental institution to commit further crimes have forced people to say that enough is enough.
Fact	The  consensus seemed to be that there must be no repetition of such situations as the one described by Chicago <ORG> Columnist <NAME> in a September 4, 1977, article about a man who had just been convicted of a particularly despicable crime."
Fact	Faced with the spectre of rising crime, people and sociologists alike, have started questioning the rehabilitation policy.
Fact	"In California another study from <ORG>, suggests that keeping habitual criminals locked up would do more to reduce crime than any rehabilitation efforts.
Fact	Despite treatment or preventive measures, habitual criminals commonly go back to crime after they are released from prison, the study showed.
Fact	In addition, the study found that deterrence to crime was in direct proportion to the relative certainty of going to jail, after being caught."
Fact	According to Encyclopaedia Britannica Year Book 1979, in 1978 also penologists were seriously divided in their views about the end of punishment.
Fact	Some penologists argued that "It is not possible  to punish and reform simultaneously": while "others would prefer to strip punishment of its moral overtones", "While many Legislators and most penologists have supported the idea that reform ought to take priority in dealing with offenders, many Judges especially in Britain and the United States, where rising crime rates are the source of much public concern have expressed grave doubts about the wisdom of this view.
Fact	They have argued that the courts must reflect a public abhorrence of crime and that justice demands that some attempt be made to impose punishment fitting to the crime".
Fact	India also, as the statistics furnished by the respondent (<ORG>) show, is afflicted by a rising rate of violent crime, particularly murder, armed robbery and dacoity etc., and this has been the cause of much public concern.
Fact	All attempts made by individual members to move Bills in the <ORG> for abolition or restriction of the area of death penalty have ended in failure.
Fact	At least four of such unsuccessful attempts we re made after India won Independence, in 1949, 1958, 1961 and 1978.
Fact	It may be noted that the last of these cttempts was only to restrict the death penalty to a few types of murders specified in the Bill.
Fact	Though it was passed by <ORG> after being recast, it has not been passed by <ORG>.
Fact	To sum up, the question whether or not death penalty serves any penological purpose is a difficult, complex and intractable issue.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has evoked strong, divergent views.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the purpose of testing the constitutionality of the impugned provision as to death penalty in Section 302 , Penal Code on the ground of reasonableness in the light of Articles 19 and 21 of the Constitution, it is not necessary for us to express any categorical opinion, one way or the other, as to which of these two antithetical views, held by the Abolitionists and Retentionists, is correct.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is sufficient to say that the very fact that persons of reason, learning and light are rationally and deeply divided in their opinion on this issue, is  a ground among others, for rejecting the petitioners argument that retention of death penalty in the impugned provision, is totally devoid of reason and purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, notwithstanding the view of the Abolitionists to the contrary, a very large segment of people, the world over, including sociologists, legislators, jurists, judges and administrators still firmly believe in the worth and necessity of capital punishment for the protection of society, if in the perspective of prevailing crime conditions in India, contemporary public opinion chanalised through the people's representatives in <ORG>, has repeatedly in the last three decades, rejected all attempts, including the one made recently to abolish or specifically restrict the area of death penalty, if death penalty is still a recognised legal sanction for murder or some types of murder in most of the civilised countries in the world, if the framers of the Indian Constitution were fully aware as we shall presently show they were of the existence of death  penalty as punishment for murder, under the Indian Penal Code , if the 35th Report and subsequent Reports of <ORG> suggesting retention of death penalty, and recommending revision of the Criminal Procedure Code and the insertion of the new Sections 235 (2) and 354 (3) in that Code providing for pre- sentence hearing and sentencing procedure on conviction for murder and other capital offences were before the <ORG> and presumably considered by it when in 1972-1973 it took up revision of the Code of 1898 and replaced it by the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973, it is not possible to hold that the provision of death penalty as an alternative punishment for murder, in Section 302 , Penal Code is unreasonable and not in the public interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	We would, therefore, conclude that the impugned provision in Section 302 , violates neither the letter nor the ethos of Article 19.
RatioOfTheDecision	We will now consider the issue whether the impugned limb of the provision in Section 302 , Penal Code contravenes Article 21 of  the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before dealing with the contention canvassed on the point, it will be proper to notice briefly the principles which should inform the interpretation of Article 21.
Precedent	In <NAME> case, which was a decision by a Bench of seven learned Judges, it was held by <NAME>, J. in his concurring judgment, that the expression 'personal liberty' in Article 21 is of the widest amplitude and it covers a variety of rights which go to constitute the personal liberty of man and some of them have been raised to the status of distinct fundamental rights under Article 19.
Precedent	It was further observed that Articles 14, 19 and 21 are not to be interpreted in water-tight compartments, and consequently, a law depriving a person of personal liberty and prescribing a procedure for that purpose within the meaning of Article 21 has to stand the test of one or more of the fundamental rights conferred under Article 19 which may be applicable in a given situation, ex-hypothesi it must also be liable to be tested with  reference to Article 14.
Precedent	The principle of reasonableness pervades all the three Articles, with the result, that the procedure contemplated by Article 21 must be 'right and just and fair' and not 'arbitrary' fancifu or 'oppressive', otherwise, it should be no procedure at all and the requirement of Article 21 would not be satisfied.
Precedent	Article 21 reads as under:  "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law."
Precedent	If this Article is expanded in accordance with the interpretative principle indicated in <NAME>, it will read as follows:  "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to fair, just and reasonable procedure established by valid law."
Precedent	223  In the converse positive form, the expanded Article will read as below:  "A peron may be deprived of his life or personal liberty in accordance with fair, just and reasonable procedure established by valid law."
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus expanded and read for interpretative purposes, Ar ticle 21 clearly brings out the implication, that the Founding Fathers recognised the right of the <ORG> to deprive a person of his life or personal liberty in accordance with fair, just and reasonable procedure established by valid law.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are several other indications, also, in the Constitution which show that the Constitution-makers were fully cognizant of the existence of death penalty for murder and certain other offences in the Indian Penal Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	Entries 1 and 2 in List III-Concurrent List-of the Seventh Schedule, specifically refer to the Indian Penal Code and the Code of Criminal Procedure as in force at the commencement of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 72 (1) (c) specifically invests the President with power to suspend, remit or commute the sentence of any person convicted of any offence, and also "in all cases where the sentence is a sentence of death".
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, under Article 161 , the Governor of a <ORG> has been given power to suspend, remit or commute, inter alia the sentence of death of any per son convicted of murder or other capital offence relating to a matter to which the executive power of the <ORG> extends.
Fact	Article 134 , in terms, gives a right of appeal to <ORG> to a person who, on appeal, is sentenced to death by <ORG>, after reversal of his acquittal by <ORG>.
Fact	Under the successive Criminal Procedure Code s which have been in force for about 100 years, a sentence of death is to be carried out by hanging.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of the aforesaid constitutional postulates, by no stretch of imagination can it be said that death penalty under Section 302 , Penal Code , either per se or because of its execution by hanging, constitutes an unreasonable, cruel or unusual punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	By reason of the same constitutional postulates, it cannot be said that the framers of the Constitution considered death sentence for murder or the prescribed traditional mode of its execution as a degrading punishment which would defile "the dignity of the individual" within the contemplation of the Pream ble to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	On parity of reasoning, it cannot be said that death penalty for the offence of murder violates the basic structure of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	224  Before we pass on to the main Question No.
RatioOfTheDecision	II, we may dispose of another contention convassed by Dr. <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is pointed out that India, as a member of <ORG>, was a participating delegate at the international conference that made the Stockholm Declaration on December 11, 1977, that India has also accepted the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights adopted by <ORG>, which came into force on March 23, 1966, and to which some 47 countries, including India, are a party.
RatioOfTheDecision	This being the position, it is stressed, India stands committed to the abolition of the death penalty.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is contended that the constitutional validity and interpretation of the impugned limb of Section 302 , Penal Code , and the sentencing procedure for capital cases provided in Section 354 (3) of the  Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973, must be considered in the light of the aforesaid Stockholm Declaration and the International Covenant, which represent the evolving attitudes and standards of decency in a maturing world.
RatioOfTheDecision	Let us examine this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	The European Convention of Human Rights came into force on September 1, 1953, and 18 countries had signed this Convention on November 4, 1950.
Precedent	India acceded to this Resolution of the Convention on March 27, 1979.
Precedent	The International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights, inter alia, provides:  " Article 6 (1) Every human being has the inherent right to life.
Precedent	This right shall be protected by law.
Precedent	No one shall be arbitrarily deprived of his life.
Precedent	(2) In countries which have not abolished the death penalty, sentence of death may be imposed only for the most serious crimes in accordance with the law in force at the time of the commission of the crime...
Precedent	It will be seen that clauses (1) and (2) of Article 6 do not abolish or prohibit the imposition of death penal ty in all circumstances.
Precedent	All that they require is that, firstly, death penalty shall not be arbitrarily inflicted; secondly, it shall be imposed only for most serious crimes in accordance with a law, which shall not be an ex post facto legislation.
Precedent	Thus, the requirements of these clauses are substantially the same as the guarantees or prohibitions contained in Articles 20 and 21 of our Constitution.
Precedent	India's commitment therefore does not go beyond what is provided in the Constitution and the Indian Penal Code and the Criminal Procedure Code .
Precedent	The Penal Code prescribes death penalty as an alternative punishment only for heinous crimes which are not more than seven in number.
Precedent	Section 354 (3) of the Criminal Procedure Code , 1973, as we shall presently discuss, in keeping with the spirit of the International Covenant, has further restricted the area of death penalty.
Precedent	India's penal laws, including the impugned provisions and their application, are thus entirely in accord with its international commitment.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will  be pertinent to note that most of the countries including those who have subscribed to this International covenant, retain death penalty for murder and certain other crimes even to the present day in their penal laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither the new interpretative dimensions given to Articles 19 and 21 by this <ORG> in <CITATION_1518037> (1) nor the acceptance by India of the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights, makes any change in the prevailing standards of decency and human dignity by which counsel require us to judge the constitutional validity of the impugned provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The International Covenant, as already noticed, does not outlaw capital punishment for murder, altogether.
RulingByPresentCourt	For all the foregoing reasons, we would answer the first main question in the negative.
RulingByPresentCourt	This takes us to Question No.
RulingByPresentCourt	II.
RulingByPresentCourt	Question No.II.
RatioOfTheDecision	Are the provisions of Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973 unconstitutional ?
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the question.
Precedent	The constitutional validity of section 354 (3) is assailed on these grou nds:  (i) (a) Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973, delegates to the <ORG> the duty to legislate the field of 'special reasons' for choosing between life and death, and ____________________ (1)  1 S.C.R.
Precedent	512.
Precedent	226  (b) permits imposition of death penalty in an arbitrary and whimsical manner in as much as it does not lay down any rational principies or criteria for invoking this extreme sanction.
Precedent	(Reliance has been placed on <NAME> v. Georgia (ibid).
Precedent	(ii) If Section 354 (3) is to be saved from the vice of unconstitutionality, the <ORG> should so interpret it and define its scope that the imposition of death penalty comes to be restricted only to those types of grave murders and capital offences which imperil the very existence and security of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	(Reliance for this argument has been placed on <NAME> case (ibid) ).
Argument	As against this, the learned Solicitor-General submits that the policy of the law in the matter of imposition of death sentence is writ large and clear in Secti on 354 (3), namely, that life imprisonment is the rule and death sentence an exception; that the correct approach should be to apply this policy to the relevant facts of the particular case, bearing on the question of sentence, and to find out if there are any exceptional reasons justifying imposition of the death penalty, as a departure from the normal rule.
Argument	It is submitted that conferment of such sentencing discretion on the courts, to be exercised judicially, in no sense, amounts to delegation of the legislative powers by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> further submits that there is no inherent impossibility in formulating broad guidelines consistent with the policy indicated by the legislature, for the exercise of the judicial functions under Section 354 (3).
RatioOfTheDecision	He emphasises that only broad guidelines, as distinct from rigid rules, can be laid down by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the discretion-proceeds the argument-is to be exercised judicially after taking into consideration all the aggravating and mitigating circumstance s relating to the crime and the criminal in a particular case, and ample safeguards by way of appeal and reference to the superior courts against erroneous or arbitrary exercise of the sentencing discretion have been provided, Section 354 (3) cannot be said to be violative of Articles 14, 19 and 21 or anything else in the Constitution, Before embarking upon a discussion of the arguments advanced on both sides, it is necessary to have a peep into the history and the legislative background of the procedural provisions relating to sentencing in the Code of criminal Procedure.
Statute	Under the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898, as it stood before its amendment by Act No.
Statute	26 of 1955, even for the seven offences mentioned earlier, which are punishable in the alternative with death, the normal sentence was the death sentence, and if the <ORG> wanted to depart from this rule, it had to give reasons for doing so.
Statute	This requirement was embodied in subsection (5) of Section 367 , which, as it then stood, was as follows: "If the  accused is convicted of an offence punishable with death and the <ORG> sentences him to any punishment other than death, the <ORG> shall in its judgment state the reason why sentence of death was not passed.
Statute	<ORG> in its 35th Report (Vol.
Statute	I), made the following comments on this provision:  "...a considerable body of opinion is in favour of a provision requiring the court to state its reasons for imposing the punishment either of death or of imprisonment for life.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, this would be good safeguard to ensure that the lower courts examine the case as elaborately from the point of view of sentence as from the point of view of guilt...It would increase the confidence of the people, in the courts, by showing that the discretion is judicially exercised.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would also facilitate the task of the High <ORG> in appeal or in proceedings for confirmation in respect of the sentence (where the sentence awarded is that of death) or in proceedings in revision for enhancement of the sentence (where the sente nce awarded is one of imprisonment of life."
RatioOfTheDecision	In deferance to this recommendation, section 66 of the Code of Criminal Procedure (Amendment) Act, 1955 (XXVI of 1955) deleted old sub-section (5) of Section 367 with effect from January 1, 1956, and thereafter, for such capital offences, it was left to the <ORG>, on the facts of each case, to pass, in its discretion, for reason to be recorded, the sentence of death or the lesser sentence.
Fact	This led to some difference of opinion whether, even after the Amendment of 1955, in case of murder the normal punishment was death or imprisonment for life (See A.I.R.
Precedent	Commentaries on the Code of Criminal Procedure, Vol.
Precedent	3, page 565, by <NAME> and <NAME>).
Precedent	Overruling its earlier decision, the Bombay High <ORG> in the <CITATION_239249> ,(1) held that death is not a normal penalty for murder.
Precedent	As against this, <ORG> of the Madras High <ORG> in Veluchami Thevar,(2) held that death was the normal punishment where there were no extenuating circumstances.
Precedent	The  third set of cases held that both the sentences were normal but the discretion as regards sentence was to be exercised in the light of facts and circumstances of the case.
Precedent	This view appears to be in accord with the decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_1384903> .
Precedent	(3) In that case, there was a clear finding by <ORG> which had been upheld by <ORG>, that each of the two appellants therein, committed a cold-blooded murder by shooting two inmates of the house simply with the object of facilitating commission of dacoity by them.
Precedent	Those persons were shot and killed even though they had not tried to put up any resistence.
Precedent	It was held by this <ORG> (speaking through Bhargava, J.)
Precedent	that in these circumstances where the murders were committed in cold-blood with the sole object of committing dacoity, the Sessions Judge had not exercised his discretion judicially in not imposing the death sentence, and <ORG> was justified in enhancing the sentence of the appellants from life imprisonment to de ath.
Precedent	<NAME> case, which we shall notice presently in further detail, proceeds on the hypothesis that even after the deletion of sub-section (5) of Section 367 in the Code of 1898, both the alternative sentences provided in Section 302 , Penal Code are normal punishment for murder, and the choice of either sentence rests in the discretion of the <ORG> which is to be exercised judicially, after taking into account all the relevant circumstances of the case.
Precedent	__________________ (1) AIR 1959 Bom.
Precedent	294 (299).
Precedent	(2) A.I.R.
Precedent	1965 Mad.
Precedent	48 at p. 49.
Precedent	(3) [1968] 3 S.C.R.
Precedent	610.
Fact	229   Section 354 (3) of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973, marks a significant shift in the legislative policy underlying the Code of 1898, as in force immediately before Apr.
Fact	1, 1974, according to which both the alternative sentences of death or imprisonment for life provided for murder and for certain other capital offences under the Penal Code , were normal sentences.
Fact	Now, according to this changed legislative policy which is patent  on the face of Section 354 (3), the normal punishment for murder and six other capital offences under the Penal Code , is imprisonment for life (or imprisonment for a term of years) and death penalty is an exception.
Fact	<ORG> in its Report, stated the object and reason of making this change, as follows:  "A sentence of death is the extreme penalty of law and it is but fair that when a <ORG> awards that sentence in a case where the alternative sentence of imprisonment for life is also available, it should give special reasons in support of the sentence"  Accordingly, sub-section (3) of Section 354 of the current Code provides:  "When the conviction is for an offence punishable with death or, in the alternative, with imprisonment for life or imprisonment for a term of years, the judgment shall state the reasons for the sentence awarded, and, in the case of sentence of death, the special reasons for such sentence."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the context, we may also notice Section 235 (2) of the Code of 1973,  because it makes not only explicit, what according to the decision in <NAME>'s case was implicit in the scheme of the Code , but also bifurcates the trial by providing for two hearings, one at the pre-conviction stage and another at the pre-sentence stage.
RatioOfTheDecision	It requires that:  "If the accused is convicted, the Judge shall, unless he proceeds in accordance with the provisions of Section 360 , hear the accused on the question of sentence, and then pass sentence on him according to law."
RatioOfTheDecision	230  <ORG> on its 48th Report had pointed out this deficiency in the sentencing procedure:  "45.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is now being increasingly recognised that a rational and consistent sentencing policy requires the removal or several deficiencies in the present system.
RatioOfTheDecision	One such deficiency is the lack of comprehensive information as to characteristics and background of the offender.
RatioOfTheDecision	The aims of sentencing:-Themselves obscure-become all the more so in the absence of information on which the correctional process is to operate.
RatioOfTheDecision	The  public as well so the courts themselves are in the dark about judicial approach in this regard.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are of the view that the taking of evidence as to the circustances relevant to sentencing should be encouraged and both the prosecution and the accused should be allowed to cooperate in the process."
RatioOfTheDecision	By enacting Section 235 (2) of the New Code, <ORG> has accepted that recommendation of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although sub-section (2) of Section 235 does not contain a specific provision as to evidence and provides only for hearing of the accused as to sentence, yet it is implicit in this provision that if a request is made in that behalf by either the prosecution or the accused, or by both, the Judge should give the party or parties concerned an opportunity of producing evidence or material relating to the various factors bearing on the question of sentence.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Of course", as was pointed out by this Court in <CITATION_819576> ,(1) "care would have to be taken by the Court to see that this hearing on the question  of sentence is not turned into an instrument for unduly protracting the proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	The claim of due and proper hearing would have to be harmonised with the requirement of expeditious disposal of proceedings."
Precedent	We may also notice Sections 432 , 433 and 433A , as they throw light as to whether life imprisonment as currently administered in ______________ (1) A.I.R.
Precedent	1976 SC.
Precedent	2286.
RatioOfTheDecision	231  India, can be considered an adequate alternative to the capital sentence even in extremely heinous cases of murder.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sections 432 and 433 of the Code of 1973 continue Sections 401 and 402 of the Code of 1898, with necessary modifications which bring them in tune with Articles 72 and 161 of the Constitution.
Statute	Section 432 invests the "appropriate Government" (as defined in sub-section (7) of that Section) with power to suspend or remit sentences.
Statute	Section 433 confers on the appropriate Government power to commute sentence, without the consent of the person sentenced.
Statute	Under clause (a) of the Section, the appropriate Government may  commute a sentence of death, for any other punishment provided by the Indian Penal Code .
Statute	With effect from December 18, 1978, the Code of Criminal Procedure (Amendment) Act , 1978, inserted new Section 433A , which runs as under :  "433A.
Statute	Restriction on powers of remission or commutation in certain cases-Notwithstanding anything contained in Section 432 , where a sentence of imprisonment for life is imposed on conviction of a person for an offence for which death is one of the punishments provided by law or where a sentence of death imposed on a person has been commuted under Section 433 into one of imprisonment for life, such person shall not be released from prison unless he had served at least fourteen years of imprisonment."
Statute	It may be recalled that in Jagmohan this <ORG> had observed that, in practice, life imprisonment amounts to 12 years in prison.
Statute	Now, Section 433A restricts the power of remission and commutation conferred on the appropriate <ORG> under Sections 432 and 433 , so that a person w ho is sentenced to imprisonment for life or whose death sentence is commuted to imprisonment for life must serve actual imprisonment for a minimum of 14 years.
Statute	We may next notice other provisions of the extent Code (corresponding to Sections 374 , 375 , 376 and 377 of the repealed Code) bearing on capital punishment.
Statute	Section 366   (i) of the Code requires the Court passing a sentence of death to submit the proceedings to <ORG>, and further mandates that such a sentence shall not be executed unless it is confirmed by <ORG>.
Statute	On such a reference for confirmation of death sentence, <ORG> is required to proceed in accordance with Sections 367 and  368.
Statute	Section 367 gives power to <ORG> to direct further inquiry to be made or additional evidence to be taken.
Statute	Section 368 empowers <ORG> to confirm the sentence of death or pass any other sentence warranted by law or to annul or alter the conviction or order a new trial or acquit the accused.
Statute	Section 369 enjoins that in ever y case so submitted, the confirmation of the sentence, or any new sentence or order passed by <ORG>, shall, when such court consists of two or more Judges, be made, passed and signed by at least two of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 370 provides that where any such case is heard before a Bench of Judges and such Judges are equally divided in opinion, the case shall be referred to a third Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this fasciculus of Sections relating to confirmation proceedings in <ORG>, the <ORG> has provided valuable safeguards of the life and liberty of the subject in cases of capital sentences.
RatioOfTheDecision	These provisions seek to ensure that where in a capital case, the life of the convicted person is at stake, the entire evidential material bearing on the innocence or guilt of the accused and the question of sentence must be scrutinised with utmost caution and care by a superior <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The High <ORG> has been given very wide powers under these provisions to prevent any possible miscarriage of justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1456106> ,  (1) this Court reiterated, with emphasis, that while dealing with a reference for confirmation of a sentence of death, <ORG> must consider the proceedings in all their aspects reappraise, reassess and reconsider the entire facts and law and, if necessary, after taking additional evidence, come to its own conclusions on the material on record in regard to the conviction of the accused (and the sentence) independently of the view expressed by the Sessions Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, where on appeal, <ORG> reverses an acquittal, and convicts the accused person and sentences him to death, Section 379 of the Code of 1973, gives him a right of appeal to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finally, there is Article 136 of the Constitution under which <ORG> is empowered, in its discretion, to __________ (1) A.I.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	1975 S.C. 1665.
RatioOfTheDecision	233  entertain an appeal on behalf of a person whose sentence of death awarded by the Sessions Judge is confirmed by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the light of the above conspectus, we will now c onsider the effect of the aforesaid legislative changes on the authority and efficacy of the propositions laid down by this <ORG> in <NAME>'s case.
RatioOfTheDecision	These propositions may be summed up as under :  (i) The general legislative policy that underlines the structure of our criminal law, principally contained in the Indian Penal Code and the Criminal Procedure Code , is to define an offence with sufficient clarity and to prescribe only the maximum punishment therefor, and to allow a very wide discretion to the Judge in the matter of fixing the degree of punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	With the solitary exception of Section 303 , the same policy permeates Section 302 and some other sections of the Penal Code , where the maximum punishment is the death penalty.
RatioOfTheDecision	(ii) (a) No exhaustive enumeration of aggravating or mitigating circumstances which should be considered when sentencing an offender, is possible.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The infinite variety of cases and facts to each case would make general standards either meaningless 'boiler plate' or a statemen t of the obvious that no Jury (Judge) would need."
RatioOfTheDecision	(Referred to <NAME> v. California(1)  (b) The impossibility of laying down standards is at the very core of the criminal law as administered in India which invests the judges with a very wide discretion in the matter of fixing the degree of punishment.
RatioOfTheDecision	(iii) The view taken by the plurality in <NAME> v. Georgia decided by <ORG>, to the effect, that a law which gives uncontrolled and un-  _____________________ (1) [1971] 402 US 183.
RatioOfTheDecision	234  guided discretion to the Jury (or the Judge) to choose arbitrarily between a sentence of death and imprisonment for a capital offence, violates the Eighth Amendment, is not applicable in India.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not have in our Constitution any provision like the Eighth Amendment, nor are we at liberty to apply the test of reasonableness with the freedom with which the Judges of <ORG> are accustomed to apply "the due process" clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are grave doubts about the expediency of  transplanting western experience in our country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Social conditions are different and so also the general intellectual level.
RatioOfTheDecision	Arguments which would be valid in respect of one area of the world may not hold good in respect of another area.
RatioOfTheDecision	(iv) (a) This discretion in the matter of sentence is to be exercised by the Judge judicially, after balancing all the aggravating and mitigating circumstances of the crime.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The discretion is liable to be corrected by superior courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exercise of judicial discretion on well-recognised principles is, in the final analysis, the safest possible safeguard for the accused.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of the above, it will be impossible to say that there would be at all any discrimination, since crime as crime may appear to be superficially the same but the facts and circumstances of a crime are widely different.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus considered the provision in Section 302 , Penal Code is not violative of Article 14 of the Constitution on the ground that it confers on the judges an unguided and uncontrolle d discretion in the matter of awarding capital punishment of imprisonment for life.
RatioOfTheDecision	(v) (a) Relevant facts and circumstances impinging on the nature and circumstances of the crime can be brought before the <ORG> at the preconviction stage, notwithstanding the fact that no formal procedure for producing evidence regarding such facts and circumstances had been specifically provided.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where counsel addresses the <ORG> with regard to the character and standing of the accused, they are duly considered by the <ORG> unless there is something in the evidence itself which belies him or the Public Prosecutor challenges the facts.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) It is to be emphasised that in exercising its discretion to choose either of the two alternative sentences provided in Section 302 , Penal Code , "the <ORG> is principally concerned with the facts and circumstances whether aggravating or mitigating, which are connected with the particular crime under inquiry.
RatioOfTheDecision	All such facts and circumstances are capable of being proved in accordance with  the provisions of the Indian Evidence Act in a trial regulated by the Cr.
Precedent	P.C.
Precedent	The trial does not come to an end until all the relevant facts are proved and the counsel on both sides have an opportunity to address the Court.
Precedent	The only thing that remains is for the Judge to decide on the guilt and punishment and that is what Sections 306(2) and 309(2) Cr.
Precedent	P.C.
Precedent	purport to provide for.
RatioOfTheDecision	These provisions are part of the procedure established by law and unless it is shown that they are invalid for any other reasons they must be regarded as valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	No reasons are offered to show that they are constitutionally invalid and hence the death sentence imposed after trial in accordance with the procedure established by law is not unconstitutional under Article 21."
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis added) A study of the propositions set out above, will show that in substance, the authority of none of them has been affected by the legislative changes since the decision in <NAME>'s case.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of course, two of them require to be adjusted and attuned t o the shift in the legislative policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first of those propositions is No.
RatioOfTheDecision	(iv) (a) which postulates, that according to the then extant Code of Criminal Procedure both the alternative sentences provided in Section 302 , Penal Code are normal sentences, and the <ORG> can, therefore, after weighing the aggravating and mitigating circumstances of the particular case, in its discretion, impose either of those sentences.
RatioOfTheDecision	This postulate has now been modified by Section 354(3) which mandates the <ORG> convicting a person for an offence punishable with death or, in the alternative with imprisonment for life or imprisonment for a term of years, not to impose the sentence of death on that person unless there are "special reasons"- to be recorded-for such sentence.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "special reasons" in the context of this provision, obviously means "exceptional reasons" founded on the exceptionally grave circumstances of the particular case relating to the crime as well as the criminal.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, the legislative policy no w writ large and clear on the face of Section 354(3) is that on conviction for murder and other capital offences punishable in the alternative with death under the Penal Code , the extreme penalty should be imposed only in extreme cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this view we are in accord with the dictum of this <ORG> in <CITATION_1981063> (1), wherein the interpretation of Section 354(3) first came up for consideration.
Precedent	After surveying the legislative background, one of us (<NAME>, J,) speaking for the <ORG>, summed up the scope and implications of Section 354 (3), thus :  "Under this provision the <ORG> is required to state the reasons for the sentence awarded and in the case of sentence of death, special reasons are required to be stated.
Precedent	It would thus be noticed that awarding of the sentence other than the sentence of death is the general rule now and only special reasons that is to say, special facts and circumstances in a given case, will warrant the passing of the death sentence.
Precedent	It is unnecessary nor is it possible to m ake a catalogue of the special reasons which may justify the passing of the death sentence in a case."
Precedent	While applying proposition (iv) (a), therefore, the <ORG> has to bear _____________________ (1) A.I.R.1976 SC 231=[1976] 2 SCR 684.
Precedent	237  in mind this fundamental principle of policy embodied in Section 354(3) .
Precedent	Another proposition, the application of which, to an extent, is affected by the legislative changes, is No.
Precedent	(v).
Precedent	In portion (a) of that proposition, it is said that circumstances impinging on the nature and circumstances of the crime can be brought on record before the pre-conviction stage.
Precedent	In portion (b), it is emphasised that while making choice of the sentence under Section 302 , Penal Code , the <ORG> is principally concerned with the circumstances connected with the particular crime under inquiry.
Precedent	Now, Section 235(2) provides for a bifurcated trial and specifically gives the accused person a right of pre- sentence hearing, at which stage, he can bring on record material or evidence, which may  not be strictly relevant to or connected with the particular crime under inquiry, but nevertheless, have, consistently with the policy underlined in Section 354(3) , a bearing on the choice of sentence.
Precedent	The present legislative policy discernible from Section 235(2) read with Section 354(3) is that in fixing the degree of punishment or making the choice of sentence for various offences, including one under Section 302 , Penal Code , the <ORG> should not confine its consideration principally" or merely to the circumstances connected with the particular crime, but also give due consideration to the circumstances of the criminal.
Precedent	Attuned to the legislative policy delineated in Sections 354(3) and 235(2) , propositions (iv) (a) and (v)  (b) in <NAME>, shall have to be recast and may be stated as below :  (a) The normal rule is that the offence of muder shall be punished with the sentence of life imprisonment.
Precedent	The court can depart from that rule and impose the sentence of death only if there are special reasons  for doing so.
Precedent	Such reasons must be recorded in writing before imposing the death sentence.
Precedent	(b) While considering the question of sentence to be imposed for the offence of murder under Section 302 Penal Code, the court must have regard to every relevant circumstance relating to the crime as well as the criminal.
Precedent	If the court finds, but not otherwise, that the offence is of an exceptionally depraved and heinous character and constitutes, on account of its design and the manner of its execution, a source of grave danger to the society at large, the court may impose the death sentence.
Precedent	The soundness or application of the other propositions in Jagmohan, and the premises on which they rest, are not affected in any way by the legislative changes since effected.
Precedent	On the contrary these changes reinforce the reasons given in Jagmohan, for holding that the impugned provisions of the Penal Code and the Criminal Procedure Code do not offend Articles 14 and 21 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, <ORG> has in Section 354(3) giv en a broad and clear guideline which is to serve the purpose of lodestar to the court in the exercise of its sentencing discretion.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> has advisedly not restricted this sentencing discretion further, as, in its legislative judgment, it is neither possible nor desirable to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> could not but be aware that since the Amending Act 26 of 1955, death penalty has been imposed by courts on an extremely small percentage of persons convicted of murder-a fact which demonstrates that courts have generally exercised their discretion in inflicting this extreme penalty with great circumspection, caution and restraint.
RatioOfTheDecision	Cognizant of the past experience of the administration of death penalty in India, <ORG>, in its wisdom, thought it best and safe to leave the imposition of this gravest punishment in gravest cases of murder, to the judicial discretion of the courts which are manned by persons of reason, experience and standing in the profession.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exercise of this sentencing discretion cannot be sai d to be untrammelled and unguided.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is exercised judicially in accordance with well-recognised principles crystalised by judicial decisions, directed along the broad contours of legislative policy towards the signposts enacted in Section 354(3) .
RatioOfTheDecision	The new Section 235 (2) adds to the number of several other safeguards which were embodied in the Criminal Procedure Code of 1898 and have been re-enacted in the Code of 1973.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then, the errors in the exercise of this guided judicial discretion are liable to be corrected by the superior courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The procedure provided in Criminal Procedure Code for imposing capital punishment for murder and some other capital crimes under the Penal Code cannot, by any reckoning, be said to be unfair unreasonable and unjust, Nor can it be said that this sentencing discretion, with which the courts are invested, amounts to delegation of its power of legislation by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument to that effect is entirely misconceived.
RatioOfTheDecision	We would, therefore, re- affirm the view taken by this C ourt in Jagmohan, and hold that the impgned provisions do not violate Articles 14, 19 and 21 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, remains the question whether this <ORG> can lay down standards or norms restricting the area of the imposition of death penalty to a narrow category of murders.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dr. <NAME> contends that the wide observations in Jagmohan as to the impossibility of laying down standards or norms in the matter of segtencing are too sweeping.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is submitted that soon after the decision in <NAME>, several States in U.S.A. amended their penal statutes and brought them in conformity with the requirements of <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Support has also been sought for this argument from <NAME> v. Georgia, wherein the Supreme <ORG> of the United States held that the concern expressed in <NAME> decision that death penalty may not be imposed in an arbitrary or capricious manner could be met by a carefully drafted statute ensuring that the sentencing authority was given adequate guidance and information for determining the appropriate se ntence, a bifurcated sentencing proceeding being preferable as a general proposition.
RatioOfTheDecision	If by "laying down standards", it is meant that 'murder' should be categorised before hand according to the degrees of its culpability and all the aggravating and mitigating circumstances should be exhaustively and rigidly enumerated so as to exclude all free-play of discretion, the argument merits rejection.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out in <NAME>, such "standardisation" is well-nigh impossible.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, there is little agreement among penologists and jurists as to what information about the crime and criminal is relevant and what is not relevant for fixing the dose of punishment for a person convicted of a particular offence.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to <NAME>, who is supposed to be the intellectual progenitor of today's fixed sentencing movement 'crimes are only to be measured by the injnry done to society'.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the 20th Century sociologists do not wholly agree with this view.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the opinion of <NAME>, the "seriousness of a crime de pends both on the harm done (or risked) by the act and degree of the actor's culpability".
RatioOfTheDecision	But how is the degree of that culpability to be measured.
RatioOfTheDecision	Can any thermometer be devised to measure its degree ?
RatioOfTheDecision	This is a very baffling, difficult and intricate problem.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, criminal cases do not fall into set- behavioristic patterns.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even within a single-category offence there are infinite, unpredictable and unforceable variations.
RatioOfTheDecision	No two cases are exactly identical.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are countless permutations and combinations which are beyond the anticipatory capacity of the human calculus.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each case presents its own distinctive features, its peculiar combinations of events and its unique configuration of facts.
Precedent	"Simply in terms of blame-worthiness or dessert criminal cases are diferent from one another in ways that legislatures cannot anticipate, and limitations of language prevent the precise description of differences that can be anticipated.
Precedent	"(1) This is particularly true of murder.
Precedent	"There is probably no offence", obs erved Sir <NAME>, Chairman of <ORG>, "that varies so widely both in character and in moral guilt as that which falls within the legal definition of murder."
Precedent	The futility of attempting to lay down exhaustive standards was demonstrated by this Court in Jagmohan by citing the instance of the Model Penal Code which was presented to <ORG> in McGoutha.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, a standardisation of the sentencing process which leaves little room for judicial discretion to take account of variations in culpability within single-offence category ceases to be judicial.
RatioOfTheDecision	It tends to sacrifice justice at the alter of blind uniformity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, there is a real danger of such mechanical standardisation degenerating into a bed of procrustean cruelty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Fourthly, standardisation or sentencing discretion is a policy matter which belongs to the sphere of legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	When <ORG> as a matter of sound legislative policy, did not deliberately restrict, control or standardise the sentencing discretion  any further than that incompassed by the broad contours delineated in Section 354 (3), _____________ (1) <NAME> and <NAME>'s Crimonology Year Book (Ibid) Albert W, <NAME>'s article at page 421.
RatioOfTheDecision	241  the <ORG> would not by over-leaping its bounds rush to do what <ORG>, in its wisdom, varily did not do.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must leave upto the <ORG>, the things that are <ORG>'s.
RatioOfTheDecision	"The highest judicial duty is to recognise the limits on judicial power and to permit the democratic processes to deal with matters falling outside of those limits".
RatioOfTheDecision	As Judges, we have to resist the temptation to substitute our own value choices for the will of the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since substituted judicial 'made-to-order' standards, howsoever painstakingly made, do not bear the peoples imprimatur, they may not have the same authenticity and efficacy as the silent zones and green belts designedly marked out and left open by <ORG> in its legislative planning for fair-play of judicial discretion to take care of the variable, unpredi ctable circumstances of the individual cases, relevant to individualised sentencing.
RatioOfTheDecision	When judges, acting individually or collectively, in their benign anxiety to do what they think is morally good for the people, take upon themselves, the responsibility of setting down social norms of conduct.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is every danger, despite their effort to make a rational guess of the notions of right and wrong prevailing in the community at large and despite their intention to abide by the dictates of mere reason, that they might write their own peculiar view or personal pre-dilection into the law, sincerely mistaking that changeling for what they perceive to be the community ethic.
RatioOfTheDecision	The perception of 'community' standards or ethics may vary from Judge to Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this sensitive, highly controversial area of death penalty, with all its complexity, vast implications and manifold ramifications, even all the Judges sitting cloistered in this <ORG> and acting unanimously, cannot assume the role which properly belongs to the chos en representatives of the people in <ORG>, particularly when Judges have no divining rod to divine accurately the will of the people.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <NAME>, the Hon'ble Judges claimed to articulate the contemporary standards of morality among the American people.
RatioOfTheDecision	But speaking through public referenda, <ORG> polls and the state legislatures, the American people sharply rebuffed them.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must draw a lesson from the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	What the learned Chief Justice, who is amongst us in this case has said recently in <CITATION_1308768> (1) in the context of laying down standards in the discre-  ________________________ (1) Criminal Appeals Nos.
RatioOfTheDecision	335 etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	of 1977 and 81 and 82 of 1978.
RatioOfTheDecision	242  tionary area of anticipatory bail, comes in as a timely reminder.
RatioOfTheDecision	In principle, these observations aptly apply to the desirability and feasibility of laying down standards in the area of sentencing discretion, also.
RatioOfTheDecision	Let us therefore, hark to the same:  "Generalisations on matters which rest on discretion and the attempt to discover formulae of  universal application when facts are bound to differ from case to case frustrate the very purpose of conferring discretion.
RatioOfTheDecision	No two cases are alike on facts and, therefore, Courts have to be allowed a little free play in the joints if the conferment of discretionary power is to be meaningful.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no risk involved in entrusting a wide discretion to <ORG> and <ORG> in granting anticipatory bail because, firstly, these are higher courts manned by experienced persons, secondly, their orders are not final but are open to appellate or revisional scrutiny and above all because, discretion has always to be exercised by courts judicially and not according to whim, caprice or fancy.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, there is a risk in foreclosing categories of cases in which anticipatory bail may be allowed because life throws up unforeseen possibilities and offers new challenges.
Precedent	Judicial discretion has to be free enough to be able to take these possibilities in its stride and to meet these challenges.
Precedent	Wh ile dealing with the necessity for preserving judicial discretion unhampered by rules of general application, <ORG> said in <NAME> and <NAME>.
Precedent	v. Rose(1).
Precedent	"I desire in the first instance to point out that the discretion given by the section is very wide.
Precedent	Now it seems to me that when the Act is so express to provide a wide discretion...it is not advisable to lay down any rigid rules for guiding that discretion.
Precedent	I do not doubt that the rules enunciated by <NAME> in the present case are useful maxims in general, and that in general they reflect the point-of view from which judges would regard an application for relief.
Precedent	But I think it ought to be distinctly understood that there may be cases in which any or all of them may be disregarded.
Precedent	If it were otherwise, the free discretion given by the statute would be fettered by limitations which have nowhere been enacted.
Precedent	It is one thing to decide what is the true meaning of the language contained ___________________________ (1) [1912] A.C. 62 3, in an Act of Parliament.
Precedent	It is quite a different thing to place conditions upon a free discretion entrusted by statute to the <ORG> where the conditions are not based upon statutory enactment at all.
Precedent	It is not safe.
Precedent	I think, to say that the <ORG> must and will always insist upon certain things when the Act does not require them, and the facts of some unforeseen case may make the <ORG> wish it had kept a free hand."
Precedent	"Judges have to decide cases as they come before them, mindful of the need to keep passions and prejudices out of their decisions.
Precedent	And it will be strange if, by employing judicial artifices and techniques, we cut down the discretion so wisely conferred upon the <ORG>s, by devising a formula which will confine the power to grant anticipatory bail within a strait-jacket.
Precedent	While laying down cast-iron rules in a matter like granting anticipatory bail, as the High <ORG> has done, it is apt to be overlooked that even Judges can have but an imperfect awareness of the needs of new situations.
Precedent	Life is ne ver static and every situation has to be assessed in the context of emerging concerns as and when it arises.
Precedent	Therefore, even if we were to frame a 'Code for the grant of anticipatory bail', which really is the business of the legislature, it can at best furnish broad guidelines and cannot compel blind adherence."
RatioOfTheDecision	From what has been extracted above, it is clear that this Court should not venture to formulate rigid standards in an area in which the <ORG> so warily treads.
RatioOfTheDecision	Only broad guidelines consistent with the policy indicated by the <ORG> in Section 354(3) can be laid down.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before we come to this aspect of the matter, it will be fair to notice briefly the decisions of <ORG> in <NAME> v. Georgia and companion cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	Soon after the decision in Furman, the Georgia <ORG> amended its statutory scheme.
Precedent	The amended statute retains the death penalty for six categories of crime: murder, kidnapping for ransom or where victim is harmed, armed robbery, rape, treason, and aircraf t hijacking.
Precedent	The statutory aggravating circumstances, the existence of any of which may justify the imposition of the extreme penalty of death, as provided in that statute, are:  "(1) The offence of murder, rape, armed robbery, or kidnapping was committed by a person with a prior record of conviction for a capital felony, (or the offence of murder was committed by a person who has a substantial history of serious assaultive criminal convictions).
Precedent	(2) The offence of murder, rape, armed robbery, or kidnapping was committed while the offender was engaged in the commission of another capital felony, or aggravated battery, or the offence of murder was committed while the offender was engaged in the commission of burglary or arson in the first degree.
Precedent	(3) The offender by his act of murder, armed robbery, or kidnapping knowingly created a great risk of death to more than one person in a public place by means of a weapon or device which would normally be hazaradous to the lives of more than one person.
Precedent	(4) The offen der committed the offence of murder for himself or another, for the purpose of receiving money or any other thing of monetary value.
Precedent	(5) The murder of a judicial officer, former judicial officer, district attorney or solicitor or former district attorney or solicitor during or because of the exercise of his official duty.
Precedent	(6) The offender caused or directed another to committed murder as an agent or employee of another person.
Precedent	(7) The offence of murder, rape, armed robbery, or kidnapping was outrageiously or want only vile horrible or inhuman in that it involved torture, depravity of mind, or an aggravated battery to the victim.
Precedent	(8) The offence of murder was committed against any peace officer, corrections employee or fireman while engaged in the performance or his official duties.
Precedent	(9) The offence of murder was committed by a person in, or who has escaped from, the lawful custody of a peace officer or place of lawful confinement.
